Thus in every proposition of pure mathematics, when fully stated, the variables have an absolutely unrestricted field: any conceivable entity may be substituted for any one of our variables without impairing the truth of our proposition.</p>\n</blockquote>\n\n<p>By W&amp;R's type theory, a number is a class of classes while <em>Socrates</em> and <em>Plato</em> are individuals. If we substitute <em>Socrates</em> and <em>Plato</em> for x and y, both the hypothesis and the consequent will be nonsense. E.g., $\\alpha \\not\\in \\alpha $ is neither true nor false: it is nonsense. And if $a$ is an individual, $\\lambda$ is a class of classes, then $a\\not\\in\\lambda$ is also nonsense. Thus, both \"Socrates and Plato are numbers,\" and \"$(Socrates + Plato)^2 = Socrates^2 + 2Socrates  Plato + Plato^2$\" are nonsense.</p>\n\n<p>When $\\phi \\supset \\psi$ is asserted, neither $\\phi$ nor $\\psi$ needs to be true, but both has to be significant. Thus, the variables in $\\phi$ and $\\psi$ must be restricted within their own types. The last sentence of §7 states that \"the variables have an absolutely unrestricted field.\" I think W&amp;R must have abandoned this view by the time they finished <em>Principia</em>. Russell's introduction to the 2nd ed of <em>The Principles</em> didn't point this out, please let me know if I am mistaken at this point.</p>\n","answer":"<p>You are right : Russell's view <em>before</em> the developmen of the Type thoery as a solution to the paradoxes was exactly that the logic was a sort of \"science of everything\", i.e. a set of laws so general to be applicable to absolutely \"everything\".</p>\n\n<p>Thus, the \"individual\" variables of what we called today <em>predicate logic</em> must range on every <em>object</em> whatever : human beings, numbers, etc.</p>\n\n<p>See e.g. :</p>\n\n<ul>\n<li>Gregory Landini, <a href=\"https://books.google.it/books?id=lE9P3fDjTYgC&amp;pg=PA31\" rel=\"nofollow\">Russell's Hidden Substitutional Theory</a> (1998), page 31.</li>\n</ul>\n"},{"link":"http://math.stackexchange.com/questions/1151631/give-a-proof-of-validity-and-indicate-the-law-used","title":"Give a proof of validity and indicate the law used","creationDate":1424148422,"checked":false,"isBad":false,"isGood":false,"site":"math","questionID":1151631,"body":"<p>Below is what I'm trying to solve, I got to a certain point but am stuck. Excluded Middle in Intuitionistic logic","creationDate":1410614652,"checked":false,"isBad":false,"isGood":false,"site":"math","questionID":929739,"body":"<p>I'm searching for a <em>intuitionistically</em> valid proof of the formula :</p>\n\n<blockquote>\n  <blockquote>\n    <p>$[((P→Q)→P)→P] ↔ (P \\lor \\lnot P)$</p>\n  </blockquote>\n</blockquote>\n\n<p>using the \"standard\" <a href=\"http://plato.stanford.edu/entries/logic-intuitionistic/\" rel=\"nofollow\">Hilbert-style axiom system</a> from Kleene [1952], for intuitionistic propositional calculus (with <em>modus ponens</em>).</p>\n\n<p>Following <a href=\"https://proofwiki.org/wiki/Peirce%27s_Law_Equivalent_to_Law_of_Excluded_Middle\" rel=\"nofollow\">Wiki</a>'s proof, I've rewritten the first part :</p>\n\n<blockquote>\n  <p>$\\vdash (P \\lor \\lnot P) → [((P→Q)→P)→P]$</p>\n</blockquote>\n\n<p>as follows :</p>\n\n<p>1) $P$ --- assumed</p>\n\n<p>2) $((P→Q)→P)→P$ --- from axiom $A → (B → A)$, with $P$ in place of $A$ and $((P→Q)→P)$ as $B$, and 1), by <em>modus ponens</em></p>\n\n<p>3) $P → [((P→Q)→P)→P]$ --- from 1) and 2) by <em>Deduction Theorem</em>, \"discharging\" assumption 1)</p>\n\n<p>4) $\\lnot P$ --- assumed</p>\n\n<p>5) $(P→Q)$ --- from axiom $\\lnot A → (A → B)$ (<em>ex falso quodlibet</em>) and 4), by <em>modus ponens</em></p>\n\n<p>6) $(P→Q)→P$ --- assumed</p>\n\n<p>7) $P$ --- from 5) and 6), by <em>mp</em></p>\n\n<p>8) $((P→Q)→P)→P$ --- from 6) and 7), by <em>Deduction Theorem</em>, \"discharging\" assumption 6)</p>\n\n<p>9) $\\lnot P → [((P→Q)→P)→P]$ --- from 4) and 8) by <em>Deduction Theorem</em>, \"discharging\" assumption 4)</p>\n\n<p>Now that we have derived : $P → [((P→Q)→P)→P]$ (3) and $\\lnot P → [((P→Q)→P)→P]$ (9), we use the axiom $(A → C) → ((B → C) → (A ∨ B → C))$ with $P$ in place of $A$, $\\lnot P$ in place of $B$ and $((P→Q)→P)→P$ as $C$ to derive, by <em>modus ponens</em> twice :</p>\n\n<blockquote>\n  <p>10) $(P \\lor \\lnot P) → [((P→Q)→P)→P]$.</p>\n</blockquote>\n\n<hr>\n\n<hr>\n\n<p><em>I'm not able to manege Wiki's proof of the other part in order to prove the conditional</em> :</p>\n\n<blockquote>\n  <p>$\\vdash [((P→Q)→P)→P] → (P \\lor \\lnot P)$.</p>\n</blockquote>\n\n<p>In Wiki's proof, as well as in the answer to this <a href=\"http://math.stackexchange.com/questions/447098/why-peirces-law-implies-law-of-excluded-middle\">post</a> we can find the proof of :</p>\n\n<blockquote>\n  <p>if $\\vdash ((P→Q)→P)→P$, then $\\vdash P \\lor \\lnot P$.</p>\n</blockquote>\n\n<p>The proof use a \"substitution instance\" of Peirce's law : $(((P \\lor \\lnot P) \\rightarrow \\bot) \\rightarrow (P \\lor \\lnot P)) \\rightarrow (P \\lor \\lnot P)$, and this is allowed only if the formula is an axiom or a theorem, which is not in <em>intuitionistic</em> logic. </p>\n\n<hr>\n\n<p>For a similar situation, see Jan von Plato, <a href=\"http://rads.stackoverflow.com/amzn/click/110761077X\" rel=\"nofollow\">Elements of Logical Reasoning</a> (2013), page 82 :</p>\n\n<blockquote>\n  <p>Given $A \\lor \\lnot A$, if $\\lnot \\lnot A$ is assumed, $A$ follows and therefore the implication $\\lnot \\lnot A \\supset A$ follows from $A \\lor \\lnot A$. An easy proof search in intuitionistic sequent calculus gives a formal derivation for :</p>\n  \n  <blockquote>\n    <p>$A \\lor \\lnot A \\supset (\\lnot \\lnot A \\supset A)$.</p>\n  </blockquote>\n  \n  <p>A similar proof search for the converse fails, for there is no derivation of :</p>\n  \n  <blockquote>\n    <p>$(\\lnot \\lnot A \\supset A) \\supset (A \\lor \\lnot A)$.</p>\n  </blockquote>\n  \n  <p>[...]</p>\n  \n  <p>The overall situation is that if $\\vdash \\lnot \\lnot A \\supset A$, then $\\vdash A \\lor \\lnot A$, but <strong>not</strong> $\\vdash (\\lnot \\lnot A \\supset A) \\supset (A \\lor \\lnot A)$.</p>\n</blockquote>\n\n<hr>\n\n<p>Thus, the <em>Question</em> is : is it possible that </p>\n\n<blockquote>\n  <blockquote>\n    <p>if $\\vdash ((P→Q)→P)→P$, then $\\vdash P \\lor \\lnot P$, </p>\n    \n    <p>but $\\nvdash [((P→Q)→P)→P] → (P \\lor \\lnot P)$</p>\n  </blockquote>\n</blockquote>\n","answer":"<p>The implication\n$$\n[((P\\rightarrow Q)\\rightarrow P)\\rightarrow P ] \\rightarrow P\\lor\\neg P \\quad\\quad\\quad\\quad (1)\n$$\nis in fact not a theorem of intuitionistic propositional logic. You can check this semantically, with a Kripke frame with two worlds $w_1\\le w_2$, with $P, Q$ both satisfied in $w_2$ and none of them satisfied in $w_1$. See <a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kripke_semantics#Semantics_of_intuitionistic_logic\" rel=\"nofollow\">here</a> for background.\nOr you could try to prove (1) in a sequent calculus and discover that you get stuck.</p>\n\n<p>What is true is that if you make Peirce's law an additional axiom, then $P\\lor\\neg P$ becomes a theorem. In symbols:\n$$\n\\{((A\\rightarrow B)\\rightarrow A )\\rightarrow A\\} \\vdash P\\lor\\neg P\n$$\nHere the curly brackets are supposed to refer to the collection of all substitution instances.\nThis is discussed in the answer you linked. (So Peirce's law and LEM are equivalent in the sense that adding either of them will give you classical logic.)</p>\n\n<p>By the deduction theorem, you then also obtain an implication similar to (1) as a theorem, but if you check what (substitution) instance of Peirce's was actually used, you'll find that $A=P\\lor\\neg P$, $B=\\bot$. So\n$$\n\\vdash [((P\\lor\\neg P\\rightarrow \\bot)\\rightarrow P\\lor\\neg P  )\\rightarrow P\\lor\\neg P] \\rightarrow P\\lor\\neg P ,\n$$\nwhich of course is not the same as (1).</p>\n\n<p>As for your last question, taken in an abstract sense, it is definitely possible to have \"$\\vdash A$ implies $\\vdash B$\" satisfied, but $A\\rightarrow B$ is not a formal theorem: the hypothesis holds whenever $\\not\\vdash A$, and there is of course no reason why this would make $A\\rightarrow B$ a theorem.</p>\n"},{"link":"http://math.stackexchange.com/questions/711023/who-stole-the-axioms-in-natural-deduction","title":"Who stole the axioms in Natural Deduction?","creationDate":1394726318,"checked":false,"isBad":false,"isGood":false,"site":"math","questionID":711023,"body":"<p>The study of Gentzen's <em>sequent calculus</em> give me the opportunity to propose some reflections about the concept of <em>logical truth</em>.</p>\n\n<p>I'll refer to the english edition of Gentzen's works : <em>The collected papers</em> (1969), with additional references to Gaisi Takeuti, <em>Proof Theory</em> (2nd ed - 1987).</p>\n\n<p><em>Sequent calculus</em> <strong>LK</strong> and <strong>LJ</strong> assume as <em>basic logical sequent</em> a sequent of the form :</p>\n\n<blockquote>\n  <blockquote>\n    <p>$A \\rightarrow A$, </p>\n  </blockquote>\n</blockquote>\n\n<p>where $A$ can be any arbitrary formula [page 151].</p>\n\n<p>A sequent of the form $A \\rightarrow A$ is called also an <em>initial sequent</em>, or <em>axiom</em> [see Takeuti]. </p>\n\n<p>We may restrict the above sequents to <em>atomic</em> formulas $A$ [see Takeuti, page 19 :  If a sequent is provable, then it is provable with a proof in which all the initial sequents consist of atomic formulas.] </p>\n\n<p>As Gentzen explain [page 82], the \"informal meaning\" of $A \\rightarrow A$ is $\\vdash A \\supset A$ [from the axiom $A \\rightarrow A$, by $\\supset$-R, we have $\\rightarrow A \\supset A$: i.e. we have a proof of $A \\supset A$].</p>\n\n<p>We have that [Takeuti, page 11] a formula $A$ is called <strong>LK</strong>-provable (or a theorem of <strong>LK</strong>) if the sequent $\\rightarrow A$ is <strong>LK</strong>-provable, where a proof <strong>LK</strong> (or <strong>LK</strong>-proof), is a tree of sequents satisfying the following conditions: </p>\n\n<p>(i) The topmost sequents are initial sequents</p>\n\n<p>(ii) Every sequent except the lowest one is an upper sequent of an inference whose lower sequent is also in the proof. </p>\n\n<p>Due to soundeness and completeness theorems for <strong>LK</strong>, we have that all and only the <em>valid</em> formulas are provable.</p>\n\n<p>If we consider the <em>basic logical sequents</em> $A \\rightarrow A$, with $A$ atomic, we may say that they are the quintessence of <em>tautology</em> (in the <strong>non</strong> technical sense of \"trivial\" logical truth).</p>\n\n<p>This suggest to me a possible way of \"reinterpreting\" some pasages from Wittgenstein's <em>Tractatus</em> (1921) :</p>\n\n<blockquote>\n  <blockquote>\n    <p>6.1 The propositions of logic are tautologies.</p>\n  </blockquote>\n</blockquote>\n\n<p>See in <em>SEP</em> <a href=\"http://plato.stanford.edu/entries/logical-truth/\">Logical Truth</a> :</p>\n\n<p>\"[Wittgenstein] claims that logical truths do not “say” anything (6.11). [...] Wittgenstein calls the logical truths analytic (6.11), and says that “one can recognize in the symbol alone that they are true” (6.113). He seems to have in mind the fact that one can “see” that a logical truth of truth-functional logic must be valid by inspection of a suitable representation of its truth-functional content (6.1203, 6.122). But the extension of the idea to quantificational logic is problematic [...]\"</p>\n\n<p>In <em>sequent calculus</em> we have also, as a corollary to the <em>cut-elimination</em> theorem, the <em>subformula property</em> [Takeuti, page 29] :</p>\n\n<blockquote>\n  <p>In a cut-free proof in <strong>LK</strong> (or <strong>LJ</strong>) all the formulas which occur in it are subformulas of the formulas in the end-sequent. </p>\n</blockquote>\n\n<p>Gentzen's \"Hauptsatz\" is often described : \"as stating that a proof can be so organized that\nthe premisses of each step of inference are always simpler [or not more complicated] than its conclusion\" [Sara Negri &amp; Jan von Plato, <em>Structural Proof Theory</em> (2001), page xii].</p>\n\n<p>This property seems to me the precise counterpart of Frege goal of the formalization :</p>\n\n<blockquote>\n  <blockquote>\n    <p>that through formalization “we arrive at a small number of laws in which, if we add those contained in the rules, the content of all the laws is included, albeit in an undeveloped state” (Frege, <em>Begriffsschrift</em>, 1879). </p>\n  </blockquote>\n</blockquote>\n\n<p>My exposition boil down to the question :</p>\n\n<blockquote>\n  <blockquote>\n    <p>May we say that <em>sequent calculus</em> support the idea that <em>logical truth</em> are the formulas provable from <em>basic logical axioms</em>, i.e. from fomulas that are \"tautologies\" (in the sense of \"trivial\" truth whose truth we may ascertain \"by inspection\") formed with \"elementary propositions\" (again Wittgenstein) ? And that these formulas are \"analytic\" (in the sense of Kant and Frege)?</p>\n  </blockquote>\n</blockquote>\n\n<p>In support of this interpretation (and this fact clarify, for me, also the interreletionship between logic and mathematics), I suggest to consider also the way in which Gentzen extends <em>sequent calculus</em> to arithmetic [page 151] :</p>\n\n<blockquote>\n  <p>A basic <em>mathematical sequent</em> is a sequent of the form $\\rightarrow C$, where the formula $C$ represents a 'mathematical axiom'.</p>\n</blockquote>\n\n<p>According to the above interpretation, the formula $C$ is <em>not</em> a \"tautology\", and the mathematical theorems proved in the <em>sequent calculus</em> with the addition of \"mathematical axioms\" are clearly nor more \"analytic\".</p>\n\n<hr>\n\n<p>It is well-known that <em>sequent calculus</em> and <em>natural deduction</em> have their common origin in Gentzen's work.</p>\n\n<p>There is a \"standard\" translation from cut-free derivations in the <em>sequent calculus</em> to natural deduction derivations [see Negri &amp; von Plato, chapter 8].</p>\n\n<p>In <em>natural derivation</em> there are no <em>axioms</em> : the said translation maps the sequent $A \\rightarrow A$ into an <em>assumption</em> $A$.</p>\n\n<p>So my final question is : </p>\n\n<blockquote>\n  <blockquote>\n    <p>What is wrong in the above interpretation of <em>logical truth</em> \"according to\" <em>sequent calculus</em> ? Is it possible to reconcile it with <em>natural deduction</em> ?</p>\n  </blockquote>\n</blockquote>\n\n<hr>\n\n<p><strong><em>Added</em></strong> March, 15</p>\n\n<p>Following some comments, It's seems that is a little bit \"improper\" to call axioms the <em>initial sequents</em>; Gentzen did not do so. He uses the term only in the context of 'mathematical axioms', i.e. for <em>basic mathematical sequent</em> [page 151].</p>\n\n<p>So we may \"restore the simmetry\" between <em>sequent calculus</em> and <em>natural deduction</em> : <em>initial sequents</em> are - exactly as <em>assumptions</em> - a way to \"bootstrap\" the inferences.</p>\n\n<p>In conclusion, <em>sequent calculus</em> (exactly as <em>natural deduction</em>) support the idea that a <em>logical calculus</em> is based on <em>inference rules</em> : in this sense, it is eminently \"formal\".</p>\n\n<p>Only when we \"apply\" it, i.e.introduce <em>mathematical</em> axioms (or axioms partaining to other sciences), we \"introduce meaning\".</p>\n\n<p>If this view is sound, it seems to me to maintain a \"strictly formal\" characterization of <em>logical truth</em>; in order to characterize it we need <em>semantics</em>, via the concept of <em>validity</em>.</p>\n","answer":"<p>I don't think there's necessarily anything deep going on here.</p>\n\n<p>Your initial observation seems to be simply that we can trade logical axioms for rules of inference and vice versa. A Hilbert-type system tries to have as few rules of inference as possible (namely modus ponens; it needs at least one in order to be able to do anything) and puts all the work in the axioms, whereas sequent calculus goes to the other extreme and does all the work with a plethora of inference rules while sticking to the simplest imaginable axiom.</p>\n\n<p>In principle it would be equally possible to phrase most of the <em>non-logical</em> axioms of a theory as rules of inference rather than axioms. For example, if we're doing Peano Arithmetic in natural deduction, we could choose to write the induction rule as\n$$\\frac{n=0\\to\\psi \\qquad (\\forall n. n=x\\to\\psi)\\to(\\forall n.n=S(x)\\to\\psi)}{\\psi}\nx\\text{ not free in }\\psi$$</p>\n\n<p>The only reason why we're not routinely doing that is that we often want to collect the non-logical axioms as \"objects of interest\" themselves, to be manipulated, put into sets and so forth, which is technically more convenient if they are formulas than if they're rules of inference.</p>\n\n<p>Thus I think the rule/axiom scale exemplified by Hilbert or Gentzen style is more or less orthogonal to the concept of \"logical truth\", taken to mean formulas that can be proved by purely <em>logical</em> means (whether these logical means are axioms or rules), without using any of the theory-specific rules/axioms.</p>\n"},{"link":"http://math.stackexchange.com/questions/696241/measure-of-induction-for-cut-elimination-in-sequent-calculus","title":"&quot;Measure&quot; of induction for cut-elimination in sequent calculus","creationDate":1393770580,"checked":false,"isBad":false,"isGood":false,"site":"math","questionID":696241,"body":"<p>I'm not very familiar with proof thoery, so I'm in trouble understanding different versions of the proof of the <em>Cut-elimination Theorem</em> for sequent calculus.</p>\n\n<p>In Sara Negri &amp; Jan von Plato, <em>Structural Proof Theory</em> (2001), page 35 :</p>\n\n<blockquote>\n  <p>the proof of admissibility of cut is by induction on the weight [amounting to the length of a formula] of the cut formula and a subinduction on the sum of heights of derivations [where the height of a derivation is the greatest number of successive applications of rules in it] of the two premisses. </p>\n</blockquote>\n\n<p>In Gaisi Takeuti, <em>Proof Theory</em> (2nd ed - 1987), page 23, a preliminary <em>Lemma</em> is proved with Proof of Lemma 5.4. We prove the Lemma </p>\n\n<blockquote>\n  <p>by double induction on the grade $g$ [where the <em>grade</em> of a formula $A$ is the number of logical symbols contained in it] and rank $r$ [where the <em>rank</em> of a thread $\\mathcal F$ containing a sequent $J$ is the number of consecutive sequents, counting upward from the upper sequent of $J$] of the proof $P$ (i.e., transfinite induction on $\\omega.g + r$).</p>\n</blockquote>\n\n<p>How to \"unwind\" Takeuti formula with Negri &amp; von Plato \"induction\" ?</p>\n","answer":"<p>The proofs are the same and only the terminology changes.</p>\n\n<p>The length of a formula (<em>weight</em> in [NvP2001]) corresponds to the number of connectives that it has (<em>grade</em> in [T1987]). This is because all connectives are binary (negation can be translated into implication to falsehood, $\\neg A\\equiv A\\to\\bot$).</p>\n\n<p>So the outer induction is the same in both proofs. Then, for each $n$ of the outer induction, both proofs use an inner induction on the <em>hight</em> / <em>rank</em> of the derivation of the formula with length or weight $n$.</p>\n\n<p><em>Hight</em> of a derivation is the maximum number of successive rules, meaning the maximum path of the proof tree of the derivation. So, having a formula of length $n$ fixed from the outer induction, say $Q$ is the sequent that concludes that formula. Then, [NvP2001] does the inner induction on the greatest path of the derivations of the premises of $Q$, meaning the greatest path of the subtree that $Q$ defines.</p>\n\n<p><em>Rank</em> of a derivation is the maximum number of consecutive sequents starting to count from a sequent $J$. But if you put $J=Q$, then you see that the hight of the derivations of the premises of $Q$ is exactly the same as the rank of whole proof $P$ with respect to $Q$. I mean, in the proof of [T1987], after having fixed a formula of grade $n$, he does the inner induction on the <em>rank</em> of whole proof $P$, where $J$ is the sequent that concludes the formula that is fixed from the outer induction (which is $Q$). Then, the <em>rank</em> of $P$ with respect to $J$ is the maximum path of the subtree that $J$ defines. This is exactly the same as the hight of the derivations of the two premises of $J$, where $J=Q$.</p>\n\n<p>When doing two inductions, you can define a double induction, meaning <strong>one</strong> induction on <strong>one</strong> variable that has parity two and on which you do the total induction. So, Takeuti says that the variable, $v$, of the total (inner and outer together) induction is $v(g,r)=\\omega\\cdot g+r$. <strong>Attention</strong> that $\\omega\\cdot g+r$ is a new <strong>variable</strong> defined on the previous two. What you stated to be $\\omega\\times\\omega$ is the <strong>space</strong> from which $v$ takes values. Actually, the expression $\\omega\\cdot g+r$ defines a hierarchy on the two inductions. In this case, it means that induction on $r$ is a subinduction of the induction on $g$. This is because $v(g,r)=\\omega g +r=g+r=\\max\\{g,r\\}$ ($\\omega g$ is the intersection of $\\omega$ and $g$, which is of course $g$ and $g+r$ is the union of $g$ and $r$, which is their maximum). So, you have variable $v$ defined as:\n$$v(g,r)=\n\\begin{cases}\ng,~\\text{ if } g\\geq  r\\\\\nr,~\\text{ if } g&lt; r\n\\end{cases}$$</p>\n\n<p>But this is the same as doing an outer induction on $g$ and then doing an inner induction on $r$, for $r\\leq g$. Because if the outer induction is on $g$, then this means that $v(g,r)=g\\Rightarrow r\\leq g$. So, then you do your inner induction on $r$.</p>\n"},{"link":"http://math.stackexchange.com/questions/690046/a-problem-about-sequent-calculus-for-classical-logic","title":"A problem about sequent calculus for classical logic","creationDate":1393346940,"checked":false,"isBad":false,"isGood":false,"site":"math","questionID":690046,"body":"<p>In Sara Negri &amp; Jan von Plato, <em>Structural Proof Theory</em> (2001), page 51, various properties of the system <strong>G3cp</strong> of classical propositional logic are showed.</p>\n\n<p><strong>Theorem 3.1.1</strong> [page 49] proves that all rules of <strong>G3cp</strong> are <em>invertible</em>.</p>\n\n<p>The following topic is about root-first proof search and <em>decomposition</em> of a sequent $\\Gamma \\Rightarrow \\Delta$, and state the following :</p>\n\n<p><strong>Lemma 3.1.2</strong>: The decomposition of a sequent $\\Gamma \\Rightarrow \\Delta$ into topsequents in <strong>G3cp</strong> is unique.</p>\n\n<p>The proof is a one-line proof :</p>\n\n<blockquote>\n  <p>\"By noting that successive application of any two logical rules in <strong>G3cp</strong> commutes.\"</p>\n</blockquote>\n\n<p>It is not clear to me what \"commute\" means in this context: I must consider first a rule $R_1$ followed by $R_2$ going from top to bottom and then reversing the order : $R_2$ followed by $R_1$ but in the reverse direction ?</p>\n\n<p>I've difficulties in figuring how to \"reverse the order\" of, for example, $L_{\\lor}$ followed by $L_{\\supset}$ ...</p>\n\n<p>Thanks</p>\n","answer":"<p>After a night's sleep, I think that the answer is easier than I've expected.</p>\n\n<p>In the classical sequent calculus <strong>G3cp</strong> we allow <strong>multisuccedent</strong> sequents $\\Gamma \\Rightarrow \\Delta$.</p>\n\n<p>In the root-first proof search, we decompose the formulas \"bottom-up\", using the invertibility property of the rules.</p>\n\n<p>When we decompose a specific formula $\\varphi$ in e.g. $\\Delta$, if we consider the formation tree for $\\varphi$, when we \"open it\" (i.e. if we consider it as $(\\lnot \\alpha)$ or $(\\alpha_1 \\lor \\alpha_2)$ or $(\\alpha_1 \\land \\alpha_2)$ and we cancel the outermost parentheses) a single <em>connective</em> will \"surfaces\"; so there is no ambiguities about the rule to be applied.</p>\n\n<p>The presence of multisuccedents means that $\\Gamma \\Rightarrow \\Delta$ can be, for example :</p>\n\n<blockquote>\n  <p>$\\Gamma \\Rightarrow \\Delta_1, \\alpha_1 \\lor \\alpha_2, \\beta_1 \\land \\beta_2$.</p>\n</blockquote>\n\n<p>When we say that \"successive application of any two logical rules in <strong>G3cp</strong> commutes\" we means simply that starting the decomposition process from $\\alpha_1 \\lor \\alpha_2$ produces the same end result that starting from $\\beta_1 \\land \\beta_2$.</p>\n"},{"link":"http://math.stackexchange.com/questions/658262/what-is-the-correct-reading-of-bot","title":"What is the &quot;correct&quot; mathematical.</p>\n\n<p><strong>First headline: $\\bot$ and $\\top$ are wellformed formulas.</strong> </p>\n\n<p>(On purpose I mention them both here because in this aspect they are the same)</p>\n\n<p>Different authors have different formulations of this fact:</p>\n\n<ul>\n<li>$\\bot$ and $\\top$ are propositional constants</li>\n<li>$\\bot$ and $\\top$ are a zero-place connectives</li>\n<li>$\\bot$ and $\\top$ are atomic formula</li>\n</ul>\n\n<p>They all point to the same thing $\\bot$ and $\\top$ can be part of a formula, it can be used like a normal propositional variable in all rules of the logic.\nso if $ ( P \\to (Q \\to R )) \\to ( (P \\to Q )\\to (P \\to R )) $ is a theorem then so are \n$ ( \\top \\to (Q \\to \\bot )) \\to ( (\\top \\to Q )\\to (\\top \\to \\bot )) $ and \n$ ( \\bot \\to (\\bot \\to R )) \\to ( (\\bot \\to \\bot )\\to (\\bot \\to R )) $ and many more,\nthey are not very helpful but that is beside the point)</p>\n\n<p>This is all about being  wellformed and how you can use them in formulas , it has nothing about what  $\\bot$ means.</p>\n\n<p>Some logics just don't define $\\bot$ or $\\top$ as a wellformed formula, so in those logics they just do not exist.</p>\n\n<p><strong>What does $\\bot$ mean?</strong> </p>\n\n<p>This is a philosophical question.</p>\n\n<p>If you see logic just as symbol manipulation (the philosophy of mathematics known as formalism) , no symbol means anything and so questioning what a particular symbol means is meaningless.</p>\n\n<p>The above is I guess not very helpful, so different logicians come up with different ideas.</p>\n\n<ul>\n<li><p>$\\bot$ means absurd: $ P  \\to \\bot$ means that P leads to absurdity ( and we don't want that)</p></li>\n<li><p>$\\bot$ means refutability: $ P  \\to \\bot$ means that P is refutable ( and so P is false)</p></li>\n<li><p>$\\bot$ means non-demonstrability $ P  \\to \\bot$ means that P is not demonstable (so not provably true)</p></li>\n</ul>\n\n<p>The above is a rewriting from \"Foundations of Mathematical logic\" Curry (1963), chapter 6 \"negation\" , the chapter goes much deeper in it, there is a dover edition of it, highly recomended, but negation is much more complex than it looks, in another article I saw, I think 7 different negations appeared, and i do doubt that article mentioned them all.</p>\n\n<p>Wittgenstein came up with \" meaning follows from use \" so maybe the only way you can find the meaning is to look at how it is used.</p>\n\n<ul>\n<li><p>If $ \\bot \\to P $ is a theorem then $\\bot$ means absurdity, it is quite absurd that every formula is true. </p></li>\n<li><p>If $ ((P  \\lor R) \\to ((P \\to\\bot) \\to R) $ is a theorem then $\\bot$ means refutability, P is refuted (and therefore R is true)</p></li>\n<li><p>If $ (P  \\lor (P \\to\\bot) ) $ you have classical logic.</p></li>\n</ul>\n\n<p>so it all depends, but can you expect anything else with a philosophical question.</p>\n"},{"link":"http://math.stackexchange.com/questions/655776/how-to-prove-consistency-of-natural-deduction-systems","title":"How to prove consistency of Natural Deduction systems","creationDate":1390991553,"checked":false,"isBad":false,"isGood":false,"site":"math","questionID":655776,"body":"<p>In Dag Prawitz, <em>Natural Deduction A Proof-Theoretical Study</em> Similarly, a connective that has once appeared in a derivation cannot disappear. From this it follows in particular that $\\implies \\bot$ is not derivable, i.e., the calculus is syntactically <strong>consistent</strong>.</p>\n</blockquote>\n\n<p>We have that [page 183] :</p>\n\n<blockquote>\n  <p>by the translation of a normal derivation in natural deduction to sequent calculus,\n  each formula in the former appears in the latter. We therefore have, by the subformula property of <strong>G0i</strong>, a somewhat surprising proof of </p>\n  \n  <p><strong>Corollary 8.3.3: Subformula property</strong>. In a normal derivation of $C$ from open\n  assumptions $\\Gamma$, each formula in the derivation is a subformula of $\\Gamma$, $C$.</p>\n</blockquote>\n\n<p>So (I think) is only after having obtained <em>normalization</em> that, from the <em>subformula property</em> we can conclude that a derivation of $\\bot$ (i.e.<em>inconsistency</em>) is possible only if $\\bot$ is a subformula of some of the premises.</p>\n\n<p>If we apply this to the \"pure\" calculus, now we have <em>consistency</em> due to the fact that a proof of $\\bot$ (i.e. $\\vdash \\bot$) would implies that $\\bot$ is a subformula of the premises; but we have no premises.</p>\n"},{"link":"http://math.stackexchange.com/questions/530792/who-gave-you-the-epsilon","title":"Who gave you the epsilon?","creationDate":1382086288,"checked":false,"isBad":false,"isGood":false,"site":"math","questionID":530792,"body":"<p><em>Who gave you the epsilon?</em> is the title of an article by J. Grabiner on Cauchy from the 1980s, and the implied answer is \"Cauchy\".  On the other hand, historian I. Grattan-Guinness points out in his book that \"Weierstrass undoubtedly saw himself as Cauchy's heir in analysis and so helped to create the belief that Cauchy's achievements included ideas that were actually his own\" (<em>The development of the foundations of mathematical analysis from Euler to Riemann</em>, page 120). In fact, Cauchy apparently never gave an epsilon-delta definition of either <em>limit</em> or <em>continuity</em>.  His infinitesimal definition of continuity (\"infinitesimal $x$-increment always produces infinitesimal $y$-increment\") is reproduced in a number of secondary studies, for example in J. Gray (<em>Plato's Ghost</em>, page 64). The question is then, who in fact gave you epsilon-delta: Cauchy or Weierstrass? Editors are requested to refrain from answers based purely on opinion, but rather to base themselves on published sources supporting either of the two views. In particular, a primary source in Cauchy giving an epsilon-delta definition of continuity would be welcome.</p>\n\n<p>Note 1. Grabiner's example cited by @Brian  illustrates the issue well. Cauchy infrequently uses preliminary forms of epsilon, delta <em>arguments</em> (notice the opening \"let delta, epsilon be very small numbers\" which would certainly not make it into calculus texts today) in some of his proofs, but he never gave an epsilon, delta <em>definition</em>. This example suggests that Cauchy never gave such a definition, for if he did, Grabiner would have likely cited it.  Cauchy's definition of continuity is \"infinitesimal $x$-increment always produces an infinitesimal change in $y$\".  This is closer to the modern infinitesimal definition of continuity than to the modern epsilon, delta definition; in fact it looks identical to the modern hyperreal definition at the syntactic level.</p>\n","answer":"<p>It was Bernard Bolzano. According to <a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/%28%CE%B5,_%CE%B4%29-definition_of_limit\">Wikipedia</a>, \"In calculus, the (ε, δ)-definition of limit ... is a formalization of the notion of limit. It was first given by Bernard Bolzano in 1817, followed by a less precise form by Augustin-Louis Cauchy.\"</p>\n"},{"link":"http://math.stackexchange.com/questions/499050/spiral-of-theodorus-discussion","title":"Spiral of Theodorus - Discussion","creationDate":1379629006,"checked":false,"isBad":false,"isGood":false,"site":"math","questionID":499050,"body":"<p>The fact that $\\sqrt2$ is not rational goes back to <a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Theodorus_of_Cyrene\" rel=\"nofollow\">Theodorus of Cyrene</a> from the school of Pythagoras, and is discussed in Plato's dialog \"Theaetetus\".</p>\n\n<p>Of course, $\\sqrt n$ is not rational for any square-free positive integer $n$.\nHowever, the theorem was stated and proved by Theodorus of Cyrene only for $n&lt;17$ unless $n=1,4,$ and $9$.</p>\n\n<p>What are some plausible explanations of the obstacle which did not allow Theodorus of Cyrene to obtain this important theorem in full generality?</p>\n\n<hr>\n\n<p>I thought that it's possibly because since he used the traditional Pythagorean method of odds and evens, $17$ is the first number for which this method breaks down.</p>\n\n<p>Or maybe that the $\\sqrt{17}$ adjacent leg belongs to the last triangle that does not overlap the figure, from the <a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral_of_Theodorus\" rel=\"nofollow\">Spiral of Theodorus</a>.</p>\n\n<p>What are some other possible reasons why he stopped at $17$?</p>\n","answer":"<p>From the Wikipedia entry for Wilbur Knorr, on one of his books:</p>\n\n<blockquote>\n  <p><em>The Evolution of the Euclidean\n  Elements: A Study of the Theory of\n  Incommensurable Magnitudes and Its\n  Significance for Early Greek\n  Geometry</em> (Dordrecht: D. It traces the early history of\n  irrational numbers from their first\n  discovery (in Thebes between 430 and\n  410 BC, Knorr speculates), through the\n  work of Theodorus of Cyrene, who\n  showed the irrationality of the square\n  roots of the integers up to 17, and\n  Theodorus' student Theaetetus, who\n  showed that all non-square integers\n  have irrational square roots. It is clear from drawing graphs that the function $x^2$ goes to zero as x tends to zero faster than $x$, for example. The analysis extends in a straightforward way to rational and even irrational power of $x$, but Thomae noted that the natural logarithm, the function $\\ln(x)$, goes very slowly to $-\\infty$ as $x$ remains positive and tends to zero, so the function $\\frac{1}{\\ln (x)}$ goes to zero very slowly as x tends to zero; slower, in fact, than any power of $x$. J. Wildberger, a finitist who seems to think that mathematics should only focus on things that have some sort of \"real world\" connection, which excludes many infinite objects in particular.  This viewpoint has some sympathy in the world of computation, for example, where algothims are a focus.  Some of these post have cause some controversy and discussion over on Meta: <a href=\"http://meta.math.stackexchange.com/questions/9139/on-the-closing-of-does-mathematics-require-axioms\">On the closing of \"Does mathematics require axioms?\"</a></p>\n\n<p>For the record, my background is traditional and formalist, with a fairly typical amount of Platoism (I think natural numbers \"exist\" as much as anything mathematical exist).</p>\n\n<p>I'd like to take a somewhat different tack to some of these issues, hopefully in a way that satisfies this site's goal of producing actual answers.  My question is:</p>\n\n<p><strong>What are some examples where using \"infinite\" or arguably \"unreal\" mathematical objects does a better job of dealing with \"real\" objects?  I think of this concept as \"idealistic approximation\": when does idealizing a system make for better estimates?</strong></p>\n\n<p>Here's my example to make this question concrete: <em>circles</em>.  Wildberger has problems with circles.  I believe one of the reasons is that, in real 2-space for example, the points of a circle would have many irrational (indeed, transcendental) coordinates, and the real numbers are a problem for him.  I'll go further: there are no actual circles in nature, best we can tell.  Anything that appears circular must have imperfections at some level of precision.  See <a href=\"http://math.stackexchange.com/questions/367378/whats-the-logical-flaw-in-euclids-construction-of-the-triangle\">this post</a> for an example.</p>\n\n<p>The counter to this view is that any approach short of using an idealized perfect circle is <em>harder</em> than a more \"realistic\" approach.  By using unreal perfect circles, we obtain beautiful simple equations (for circumference, area, etc) that can be used for actual computations, which can be converted back to rational estimates to any desired degree of precision.  To try and address circles \"realistically\" from the start, taking into account their imperfections, is needlessly complex: it would inhibit understanding rather than aid it.</p>\n\n<p>Another example question of this type asked for real world instances of limits, <a href=\"http://math.stackexchange.com/questions/365770/what-is-a-simple-example-of-a-limit-in-the-real-world/368873#368873\">here</a>.</p>\n\n<p>The point is that finitist math is already embedded inside traditional math.  Finitists seem to think that the math outside of finitist hinders pedagogy.  I'd like the answers to this question to provide some counterexamples to this view.</p>\n","answer":"<p>One example that's close to my field is how much simpler <a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dynamical_systems_theory\" rel=\"nofollow\">dynamical systems theory</a> is in continuous time than in discrete time.  For example, the <a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Poincar%C3%A9%E2%80%93Bendixson_theorem\" rel=\"nofollow\">Poincaré–Bendixson theorem</a> shows that continuous-time dynamical systems in less than three dimensions can never exhibit chaos; discrete-time dynamical systems can be chaotic in any number of dimensions, even just one.</p>\n"},{"link":"http://math.stackexchange.com/questions/270068/mathematics-philosophy-and-writing","title":"Mathematics, Philosophy and writing.","creationDate":1357266575,"checked":false,"isBad":false,"isGood":false,"site":"math","questionID":270068,"body":"<p>Do you know of any famous mathematicians who were also philosophers? I don't know of any geometric connection but I would expect it to be very subtle, not as direct as what you are suggesting.  The fact that there is both a 2-3-4 triangle and a 6-6-6 triangle is a coincidence emboldened by the choice of a relatively large base.</p>\n"},{"link":"http://math.stackexchange.com/questions/101768/are-half-of-all-numbers-odd","title":"Are half of all numbers odd?","creationDate":1327351852,"checked":false,"isBad":false,"isGood":false,"site":"math","questionID":101768,"body":"<p>Plato puts the following words in Socrates' mouth in the <em>Phaedo</em> dialogue:</p>\n\n<blockquote>\n  <p>I mean, for instance, the number three, and there are many other examples. Take the case of three; do you not think it may always be called by its own name and also be called odd, which is not the same as three? Yet the number three and the number five and <strong>half of numbers in general are so constituted, that each of them is odd</strong> though not identified with the idea of odd. And in the same way two and four and all the other series of numbers are even, each of them, though not identical with evenness. (<a href=\"http://www.perseus.tufts.edu/hopper/text?doc=Perseus%3atext:1999.01.0170%3atext=Phaedo%3asection=104a\">104a-b</a>)</p>\n</blockquote>\n\n<p>The <a href=\"http://philosophy.stackexchange.com/q/2148/73\">philosophical point</a> is that there exists an Idea (or Form) called Odd and odd numbers are merely specific instances of Odd.  The are not, themselves, identical to the concept of Oddness.</p>\n\n<p>But I bolded a throwaway phrase that prompts my question: Are half of all integers odd?</p>\n\n<hr>\n\n<p>Plato likely did not consider <a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Parity_%28mathematics%29#History\">one to be odd</a> nor did he likely consider either zero or negative integers among his set of \"numbers in general\".  I don't see his proof (if he had or was aware of one), but I would imagine it be something along the lines that for every even number <code>N</code> there is an odd number <code>N+1</code>.  Therefore half of all numbers greater than 1 are odd.</p>\n\n<p>I'm aware that <a href=\"http://math.stackexchange.com/q/15556/23504\">zero is even</a>, which makes me think there is one extra even number than odd numbers.  My thinking is that if half of all positive numbers are odd and half of all negative numbers are odd, than leaving out zero, half of all integers are odd.  But when you add in the only non-positive, non-negative number, which is even, you have an extra even number.  (The programmer in me wants to add the concepts of <code>-0</code> and <code>+0</code> for symmetry.  The rest of me thinks that's nuts!)</p>\n\n<p>However, I think my imagined platonic proof still works as long as zero is definitely even and won't work if it's either odd or neither parity.</p>\n\n<p>Are either of these proofs valid?</p>\n","answer":"<p>I actually think that there are good reasons that show that half of all integers are odd.  If you ask a lot of mathematicians this question, they'd give you some silly example showing you that, for example, only $\\frac{1}{1000}$ of all numbers are odd.  They'd do this by constructing a bijection like the following mapping every odd number to a distinct sequence of $999$ even numbers: </p>\n\n<p>$$\n\\phi(1)=(2,4,6,8,\\dots,1998) \\\\\n\\phi(3)=(2000,2002,\\dots,3998) \\\\\n\\dots\n$$</p>\n\n<p>However, this approach only uses the <em>combinatorial</em> structure of the integers, and ignores the <em>algebraic</em> structure.  If we require $\\phi$ to be not just a bijection from the odd numbers to some sequence of even numbers, but instead to be a group homomorphism whose kernel is the even numbers, we have only one choice: </p>\n\n<p>$$\n\\phi(n)=n\\mod 2\n$$</p>\n\n<p>For finite groups, we know that $|G|/|\\ker\\phi| = |\\phi(G)|$.  If we pretend that this applies to infinite groups as well, then that means that there are twice as many integers as there are even numbers, since the image of our homomorphism is the two-element group $\\mathbb{Z}_2$.  Therefore, half of all integers are odd.  </p>\n\n<p>Another approach we could take is to use the orbit-stabilizer theorem, which states that for a finite group $G$, a set $X$ and an action $\\psi:G\\times X\\to X$, we have $|G|=|\\mathrm{orb}_\\psi(x)||\\mathrm{stab}_\\psi(x)|$ for any $x\\in X$, where $\\mathrm{orb}_\\psi(x)$ is the orbit of $x$ under $\\psi$ and $\\mathrm{stab}_\\psi(x)$ is the stabilizer of $x$ under $\\psi$.  If we pretend this applies to infinite groups, we can take $G=\\mathbb{Z}$, $X=\\{\\{\\textrm{odd numbers}\\}, \\{\\textrm{even numbers}\\}\\}$, and let $G$ act on $X$ by addition.  Then the orbit of the set of odd numbers is the whole of $X$, which has size $2$, and the stabilizer of that set is precisely the set of even numbers.  So we get </p>\n\n<p>$$\n|\\mathbb{Z}| = 2\\left|\\{\\textrm{even numbers}\\}\\right|\n$$</p>\n\n<p>and therefore, half of all integers are odd.  </p>\n\n<p>Another, more combinatorial approach, is as follows.  We might not want to take full advantage of the algebraic structure of $\\mathbb{Z}$, but the example at the top showing that the odd numbers occupy only $\\frac{1}{1000}$ of the integers seems silly for a nother reason: the images of the odd numbers $\\phi(n)$ are too far away from the odd numbers themselves!  One way of using this is to define the notion of density, which has been done above.  Consider instead the following approach.  Let the integers be the vertices of a graph, and join two integers if they are adjacent.  So $2$ and $3$ are joined by an edge, while $2$ and $4$ are not.  (This makes sense, because adjacent numbers are in some sense close together.  It would be silly to connect the number $3$ to the number $2000$.)  </p>\n\n<p>Then this graph is bipartite, and its vertex sets are precisely the set of odd numbers and the set of even numbers.  Moreover, the graph is regular, as each vertex has precisely two neighbours.  A simple counting argument shows that all finite regular bipartite graphs have vertex sets that are the same size.  <em>If we pretend that this applies to infinite regular bipartite graphs</em> (notice a theme here?) then we see that the set of odd numbers and the set of even numbers are the same size, so half of all integers are odd.  </p>\n"},{"link":"http://philosophy.stackexchange.com/questions/25339/how-to-self-study-plato","title":"How to self study Plato?","creationDate":1438706642,"checked":false,"isBad":false,"isGood":false,"site":"philosophy","questionID":25339,"body":"<p>I started to self study Plato but I am not sure how to arrange my knowledge. In general philosophy books, Plato's philosophy is separated by subject matter (metaphysics, language, ethics), but this can be difficult to sort out when I'm reading his dialogues. Basically, should I be sorting my knowledge by each dialogue or by subject matter? Thanks.</p>\n"},{"link":"http://philosophy.stackexchange.com/questions/25278/can-colour-be-considered-as-an-aspatial-and-atemporal-universal","title":"Can colour be considered as an aspatial and atemporal universal?","creationDate":1438333868,"checked":false,"isBad":false,"isGood":false,"site":"philosophy","questionID":25278,"body":"<p>In physics, mass is a universal; one unit of mass is the same yesterday as it is today; as it is here or orbiting Betelgeuse; and if we accept the atomistic thesis then this result follows from the invariance of number; as implicitly pointed out in Lucretious <em>On Nature</em>. </p>\n\n<p>Now consider a man holding a red apple; he sees before him a <em>red</em> apple. </p>\n\n<p>Translate him in space; to Mars say; and ask him what he sees before him - it is still a <em>red</em> apple, he remarks (of course the lighting conditions that normatively obtain on Earth have to be set). </p>\n\n<p>Translate him to Alpha Centauri, or to a small comet orbiting Betelgeuse: the same observation obtains; etc, etc.</p>\n\n<p>Consider now translating him in time, to yesterday, or yesteryear or a thousand years ago; and again with the proviso that lighting conditions are made as on earth, he still remarks that the red apple in his apple still looks red, the same colour of red as before. </p>\n\n<p>Thus, redness is a <em>universal</em>; but a universal that is spatial and temporal; and this is in line with Kants thesis that space and time are the conditions of experience. </p>\n\n<p>But is it possible that redness as a universal can be a platonic form/essence (I'm not sure of the correct terminology here)? That is exist outside of space and time? </p>\n"},{"link":"http://philosophy.stackexchange.com/questions/24864/perception-of-the-platonic-realm","title":"Perception of the Platonic realm","creationDate":1436295124,"checked":false,"isBad":false,"isGood":false,"site":"philosophy","questionID":24864,"body":"<p>If the Platonic realm is to exist, can it be perceived if it is to be purely isolated by itself? I believe I'm understanding what Socrates is saying; however I do not think it is correct.</p>\n\n<p>Referring to 10d to 11b</p>\n\n<p>Socrates forces Euthyphro make a definition 'piety'. Then, Euthyphro, quite carelessly, says 'Something loved by all gods is pious.'</p>\n\n<p>Socrates questions this statement in a lot ways to point out that, in fact, Euthyphro knows nothing about so-called piety.</p>\n\n<p>One of the methods that Socrates chose is to show that 'god-loved' and 'pious' are logically not equal. In order to that, first, Socrates persuades Euthyphro (and me) that:</p>\n\n<blockquote>\n  <p>It is not being loved by those who love it because it is something loved,\n  but it is something loved because it is being loved by them</p>\n</blockquote>\n\n<p>What I understand from this statement is that Socrates is suggesting that something is <em>loved</em> because there exists something that <em>loves</em> it. So a necessary condition for <strong>A</strong> to be <em>loved</em> is that there exists something that <em>loves</em> <strong>A</strong>. It would be false to say something <em>loves</em> <strong>A</strong> because it is <strong>inherently</strong> loved. That makes sense to me.</p>\n\n<p>Socrates continues:</p>\n\n<blockquote>\n  <p>Then <strong>the god-loved is not the same as the pious</strong>, Euthyphro, nor the pious the same as the god-loved, as you say it is, but one\n  differs from the other.</p>\n</blockquote>\n\n<p>Socrates defends his statement:</p>\n\n<blockquote>\n  <p><strong>Because we agree that the pious is being loved for this\n  reason, that it is pious</strong>, but it is not pious because it is being loved. Is\n  that not so? And that the god-loved, on the other hand, is so because\n  it is being loved by the gods, by the very fact of being loved, but it is\n  not being loved because it is god-loved.</p>\n</blockquote>\n\n<p>He, then, concludes:</p>\n\n<blockquote>\n  <p>But if the god-loved and the pious were the same, my\n  dear Euthyphro, then if the pious was being loved because it was pious,\n  the god-loved would also be being loved because it was god-loved; and\n  if the god-loved was god-loved because it was being loved by the gods,\n  then the pious would also be pious because it was being loved by the\n  gods. <strong>But now you see that they are in opposite cases as being altogether\n  different from each other: the one is such as to be loved because it is\n  being loved, the other is being loved because it is such as to be loved.</strong></p>\n</blockquote>\n\n<p>Here Socrates says that if <strong>B</strong> is <em>pious</em>, then <strong>B</strong> is <em>god-loved</em>. (Socrates says something is loved because it is pious); but, according to him, we cannot say if <strong>B</strong> is <em>god-loved</em>, then it is <em>pious</em>.</p>\n\n<p>I do not think this statement is similar to that statement said by him earlier (my first quote). Let me name statements said by Socrates:</p>\n\n<p><strong>P</strong>:</p>\n\n<blockquote>\n  <p>Something is not being loved by those who love it because it is something loved; but it is something loved because it is being loved by them</p>\n</blockquote>\n\n<p><strong>Q</strong>:</p>\n\n<blockquote>\n  <p>Pious is being loved because it is pious; but it is not pious because it is being loved.</p>\n</blockquote>\n\n<p><strong>R</strong>:</p>\n\n<blockquote>\n  <p>God-loved, is so because it is being loved by the gods, by the very fact of being loved, but it is not being loved because it is god-loved.</p>\n</blockquote>\n\n<p>My point: I do not agree with Socrates that statement <strong>Q</strong> is a consequence of <strong>P</strong>. I agree with <strong>P</strong> and I also agree that <strong>R</strong> is a natural consequence of <strong>P</strong>. But I cannot see what does <strong>Q</strong> have to do with <strong>P</strong>.</p>\n\n<p>To me, it is quite logical to define \"pious\" as \"god-loved\" provided \"god-loved\" is explained well. In this case the necessary and satisfactory condition of <em>god-loved</em> is to be loved by all gods. So if we say something is pious if and it is god-loved; we conclude: if something is loved by all gods, then it is pious. I cannot see the logical mistake here.</p>\n\n<p>Please bear in mind that, I think previous attempts of Socrates are valid. Euthyphro fails to explain why are there wars between gods. If there are wars between them, then we can find something loved by some gods, disliked by other. This is a very valid point. But we can still define \"pious\" as \"something loved by all gods\". This itself is not erroneous unlike what Socrates says. </p>\n\n<p><strong>References:</strong></p>\n\n<ul>\n<li>All quotes are directly quoted from <em>Five Dialogues - Euthyphro, Apology, Crito, Meno, Phaedo</em>, Plato, Second Edition, Translated by G. M. A. Grube, Revised by John M. Cooper.</li>\n<li>All emphasis are mine.</li>\n</ul>\n"},{"link":"http://philosophy.stackexchange.com/questions/24468/linguistic-realism","title":"Linguistic realism","creationDate":1434397465,"checked":false,"isBad":false,"isGood":false,"site":"philosophy","questionID":24468,"body":"<p>The better way to phrase it is: \"Are there objective truths about language?\" -- this question is parallel to the question of moral realism: \"Are there objective moral truths\"?</p>\n\n<p>One way to interpret moral subjectivity is that it implies that \"what people consider to be moral, is moral.\". My analog for language is that \"what constitutes language is just how people speak\".  This would be subjective in the sense that if everyone started behaving differently then language would change -- language is just what people do when they communicate.</p>\n\n<p>On the other hand, linguistic realism would imply that there are facts or features of language that exist independent of anyone's actual speech -- maybe something like Plato's forms: there is Platonic Language, and then human languages are its shadows. </p>\n\n<p>I know this is probably not settled, but I'm not even sure of who has addressed it, so, mainly I'm looking of indications of the main schools of thought, and outlines of their rationales. The Idea is nothing else than that which is known to us more familiarly by the name of the Universal, regarded, however, not as the formal Universal, which is only a property of things, but as implicitly and explicitly existent, as reality, as that which alone is true. We translate eidoς first of all as species or kind; and the Idea is no doubt the species, but rather as it is apprehended by and exists for Thought. Of course when we understand by species nothing but the gathering together by our reflection, and for convenience sake, of the like characteristics of several individuals as indicating their distinguishing features, we have the universal in quite an external form.</p>\n</blockquote>\n\n<p>While not very explicit, I suppose we may take the singular <em>Idea</em> in the second sentence as being Hegel's concept. My question is if this sentiment is consistent with Plato's particular form of idealism. </p>\n\n<p>Already in Plato's time it came in conflict with existing mathematical practice. Plato mocked in Republic the appearence of \"becoming\" in the way geometers operated \"<em>the science itself is utterly opposed to the proofs as stated in it by its practitioners... They speak, I suppose, very laughably and perforce, for they mention squaring, applying and adding, and state all their claims as if they are engaged in action and fashioning all their proofs for the sake of action.</em>\" According to Plutarch he also came out very strongly against the use of motion, mechanically generated curves in particular, by Archytas and Eudoxus, \"<em>for thus is destroyed and corrupted the good of geometry which recurs again to sensibles and neither ascends nor lays hold of eternal and incorporeal images</em>\". Motion was acceptable to \"save the phenomena\" only, not in pure mathematics as such.</p>\n\n<p>With the notable exception of Euclid Greek geometers largely ignored Plato's proscriptions on motion, mechanical curves proliferated (Archimedean spiral, etc.). Plato's idea that the language of becoming was \"perforce\" was disputed by Menaechmus, the founder of conic sections theory, who in a <a href=\"http://www.pdcnet.org/pdc/bvdb.nsf/purchase?openform&amp;fp=ancientphil&amp;id=ancientphil_1983_0003_0001_0012_0029&amp;onlyautologin=true\" rel=\"nofollow\">debate</a> with Plato's successor Speusippus implied that geometers meant what they said. Indeed, ideal realm is at odds with hierarchical organization of mathematics by constructing complex objects from simpler ones that clearly appears even in Euclid. What possible status would mathematical constructions have for eternally co-existent unchanging objects? Since then calculus \"brought motion into mathematics\", and hierarchical constructions \nare essential in shaping the structure even of pure mathematics, and part of its appeal, rather than \"perforce\" figures of speech.</p>\n\n<blockquote>\n  <p>So was Plato misapplying his philosophy, and in itself it does not lead to his conclusions about the mathematical practice?  Or is Platonist self-identification a misnomer, and mathematicians are really sympathetic to some other kind of idealism? Were the motion and mathematical constructions discussed in the context of Platonism post-Renaissance? Can consistent Platonism be reconciled with modern mathematics at all?</p>\n</blockquote>\n\n<p>EDIT: I'd like to clarify that mathematical construction is not meant in the narrow sense of constructivism, although those certainly qualify. But modern mathematics is also full of highly non-constructivist constructions, like Cantor's generation of ordinals and cardinals, or maximal ideals, algebraic closures, and everything else involving the axiom of choice. The whole architecture of pure mathematics is based on this, relations and functions are constructed from sets, groups, posets, etc. are sets with relations and functions, then there are spaces of functions on them, operators and functionals on those, and on and on. </p>\n\n<p>Agreeing with Plato seems to diminish the import of these hierarchic constructions, if not dismiss them as chatter. After all, proofs are about reducing facts about complex structures to simpler pieces, not \"ascending and laying hold\" of their \"eternal and incorporeal images\" in their unmoving finality.</p>\n"},{"link":"http://philosophy.stackexchange.com/questions/20048/does-any-nihilist-philosopher-nevertheless-place-value-on-synthesizing-nihilit","title":"Does any &quot;nihilist&quot; philosopher nevertheless place value on synthesizing nihility","creationDate":1420599179,"checked":false,"isBad":false,"isGood":false,"site":"philosophy","questionID":20048,"body":"<blockquote>\n  <p>Theodor Adorno opens his treatise on negative dialectics with the\n  statement that \"[it] is a phrase that flouts tradition. However, there seems to be a difference between post factum regularities, like the Titius-Bode rule of planetary distances, and strict laws like universal gravity that operate without exception insinuating themselves into every occurence that falls under them. Even when they break down it is not through miraculous exceptions, but by being subsumed under another equally strict law. They manifest in a way suggesting that they somehow \"constitute\" reality rather than reflect its regularities.</p>\n\n<p>Unfortunately, the Stanford article is more about defining what laws \"are\" than explaining how they might operate. There are two classical explanations, Kant's and Plato's. On Kant's account strict laws are inserted into phenomena by our mental faculties. It is hard to maintain today as a general explanation, if anything relativistic and quantum laws operate despite our mental a priori, not because of them. And even universal gravity according to Kant is empirical, not a priori. </p>\n\n<p>Plato's view is of course that laws are ideas governing other ideas in the realm of ideas, and real things \"imitate\" ideas. \"Back to Plato\" movement got some momentum lately. <a href=\"http://plato.stanford.edu/entries/laws-of-nature/#Uni\" rel=\"nofollow\">Armstrong, Dretske and Tooley</a> describe laws as relation among universals, which link properties of particulars. But it appears they replace imitation with \"then a miracle occurs\", which relates universals to particulars, or to us for that matter.</p>\n\n<p>According to Hillary Putnam, \"realism is the only philosophy that does not make the success of science a miracle\". But on Humean account of laws as <a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brute_fact\" rel=\"nofollow\">brute facts</a> realism appears to lose its explanatory power. The exceptionless lack of miracles is itself a miracle, we might as well accept the brute fact of scientific success. If the <a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brute_fact\" rel=\"nofollow\">miracle argument</a> is taken seriously it leads much further than generic realism. Isn't Platonism the only philosophy that does not make the success of realism a miracle? </p>\n\n<blockquote>\n  <p>Is the miracle argument valid in its inference to \"best explanation\" for the laws of nature? If so, is there a way to avoid \"miracles\" without resorting to Platonism, or at least to provide a credible replacement for imitation? What are the modern non-realist accounts of the operation of natual laws and success of science in discovering them?</p>\n</blockquote>\n"},{"link":"http://philosophy.stackexchange.com/questions/19546/are-we-being-controlled-and-created-by-something-or-someone-in-the-4th-dimension","title":"Are we being controlled and created by something or someone in the 4th dimension?","creationDate":1418533801,"checked":false,"isBad":false,"isGood":false,"site":"philosophy","questionID":19546,"body":"<p>To add details to this question: Is this 3 dimension reality some kind of a mirror of the 4th dimension? Indeed, both mathematics and philosophy permeate all empirical sciences to varying degrees, in fact to complementary degrees it seems. The more there is of mathematics in a field the less there is of philosophy (sadly).</p>\n\n<p>Much is made about the difference with empirical sciences. However, if one believes in the Platonic realm and some means of accessing it directly (like Gödel did) then both mathematics and philosophy become \"empirical\" sciences of that. Husserl gave an account of ideal intuition that fulfills both conditions and is free of fantastic elements in Plato. So this distinction seems to rest on philosophical preferences. </p>\n\n<p>One could also argue that proof standards in mathematics differ only in quantity rather than quality from soundness standards for theoretical arguments in empirical sciences. Another issue often brought up is experimental falsification. But from Kuhn we know that theories are not exactly falsified by individual experiments, they fall if they broadly fail. That isn't that different from mathematics and philosophy, although mathematical theories or philosophical systems are not \"verified\" or \"falsified\" fruitful ones are perpetuated (like Lebesgue measure theory or Kant's critical method), and unfruitful ones are abandoned and become historical curiosities (like classical invariant theory or Berkeley's solipsism). For mathematics the opposite sides are argued <a href=\"http://andrewlias.blogspot.com/2004/08/is-mathematics-science.html\" rel=\"nofollow\">here</a> and in the top answer to <a href=\"http://philosophy.stackexchange.com/questions/14818/is-mathematics-considered-a-science\">Is Mathematics considered a science?</a>\nArgument about philosophy is mentioned <a href=\"http://chronicle.com/blogs/brainstorm/is-philosophy-a-science/45795\" rel=\"nofollow\">here</a>.</p>\n\n<p>Not surprisingly, Husserl wanted philosophy to be a science, even a \"rigorous\" science. Ironically, it is analytic philosophy that comes closest to fulfilling his wish. However, even he distinguishes formal and empirical sciences (corresponding to his formal and regional ontologies), and places mathematics and philosophy with the former. On the other hand, most existensialists would have none of that. Even grouping mathematics with philosophy is controversial, in academia mathematics is usually put together with sciences, and philosophy with humanities. However, even some existentialists would probably claim that they study something more fundamental than \"human culture\" (Heidegger comes to mind).</p>\n\n<blockquote>\n  <p>Is there something to the question that does not reduce to an argument about words? Accepting Husserl's formal/empirical terminology can the underlying common of \"science\" be identified without including \"pseudo-sciences\" (or should some of them be included)? And if mathematics and philosophy are (are not) sciences should they be (not be)? In case of the negative answer, how is the role of mathematics and philosophy similar/different to that of \"sciences\"?</p>\n</blockquote>\n"},{"link":"http://philosophy.stackexchange.com/questions/17802/how-does-the-modern-notion-of-empiricism-stack-up-against-the-platonist-and-ne","title":"How does the modern notion of &#39;empiricism&#39; stack up against the Platonist and Neoplatonist concept of mind and understanding above sense and opinion?","creationDate":1414361676,"checked":false,"isBad":false,"isGood":false,"site":"philosophy","questionID":17802,"body":"<p>That is to say, the interpretation of <strong>visual-auditory-tactile-kinesthetic</strong> (VATK) perception (the empiric)--usually translated, in modern gestalt theory, as <em>form</em> or <em>shape</em>--should take a more holistic approach.  By extrapolation, this--<em>form</em> or <em>shape</em>--emphasized by Plato, but meant as--\"that which is above sense and opinion (deriving from the empiric)\"--marks an 'upper-story' in a more comprehensive understanding of our world.  F.A. Schaeffer may have written much on this subject. There are several interesting features. First, these alternative theories weren't comprehensive, they only covered a limited range of phenomena, even quantum mechanics excludes relativity and quantum field theory. Second, with the exception of the theories of light they are mathematically equivalent, in other words, it's not that different mathematics describes the same physics, but rather that the same mathematics admits vastly different interpretations. </p>\n\n<p>One could say therefore that the theories are \"isomorphic\", and the interpretational differences are \"unphysical\" and irrelevant. So the answer also depends on the meaning of \"determine\". However, for something \"unphysical\" physicists spent a great deal of energy arguing about these interpretational differences (and still do in the case of quantum mechanics). One of the reasons may be that such theories are expected to produce different predictions when extended to a wider range of phenomena, at which point the difference will become physical. </p>\n\n<blockquote>\n  <p>Is it possible to have different ontologies that account for all phenomena? They could postulate existence of different objects, give different explanations for the same events, have different moral and religious implications, etc. What is the value of interpretational parts in theories if they have no effect on predictions? Multiple ontologies would seem to turn them into comforting illusions used for didactic purposes. How do different philosophical traditions address this issue? Plato's and Aristotle's views were recently discussed <a href=\"http://philosophy.stackexchange.com/questions/16093/does-mathematics-apply-to-physics-in-one-way-or-multiple-ways/16128#16128\">here</a>.</p>\n</blockquote>\n\n<p>EDIT: I realized belatedly that I didn't say enough to make the question non-trivial. There are uninteresting ways to multiply ontologies by adding decorations to them, objects that have no effect on phenomena like ether was, or by ontologizing different representations of the same mathematics, like choosing different \"centers of the world\" in astronomy. Fortunately, the answers offered more substantive sources of multiplicity and I enjoyed reading all of them. The one I accepted was the one more educational for me personally.</p>\n"},{"link":"http://philosophy.stackexchange.com/questions/16093/does-mathematics-apply-to-physics-in-one-way-or-multiple-ways","title":"Does mathematics apply to physics in one way or multiple ways?","creationDate":1411403076,"checked":false,"isBad":false,"isGood":false,"site":"philosophy","questionID":16093,"body":"<p>Does mathematics apply to physics in one way or multiple ways? What do Aristotle and Plato think?</p>\n\n<ul>\n<li><p>It would seem that Aristotle thinks mathematics can be applied to\nphysics in one way only because, for him, mathematics is abstracted\nfrom physical objects, and isn't abstraction unique? Cf. <a href=\"http://dhspriory.org/thomas/BoethiusDeTr.htm#53\">this</a>.\nHowever, <a href=\"http://dhspriory.org/thomas/DeCoelo.htm#2-17\"><em>In II De cælo</em> lect. 17</a> n. 451, St. Thomas Aquinas's\ncommentary on Aristotle's <a href=\"https://ebooks.adelaide.edu.au/a/aristotle/heavens/book2.html\"><em>On the Heavens</em> bk. 2</a>, portrays\nAristotle as thinking that mathematics could be applied to physics in many\nways. St. Thomas writes that astronomers have</p>\n\n<blockquote>\n  <p>tried to reduce the irregularities [of the planets] to a right order\n  by assigning diverse motions to the planets…Yet it is not necessary\n  that the various suppositions which they hit upon be true—for although\n  these suppositions save the appearances, we are nevertheless not\n  obliged to say that these suppositions are true, because perhaps there\n  is some other way men have not yet grasped by which the things which\n  appear as to the stars are saved. Aristotle nevertheless uses\n  suppositions of this kind, in what regards the quality of the motions,\n  as true.</p>\n</blockquote>\n\n<p>The \"suppositions\" mentioned are mathematical-physical theories, like Ptolemy's theory or, more recently, Copernicus's.</p></li>\n<li><p>Plato would seem to think mathematics can be applied to physics in\nmultiple ways because he invented the phrase \"<a href=\"http://books.google.com/books?id=UofBybolmREC&amp;lpg=PP1&amp;pg=PA131#v=onepage&amp;q&amp;f=false\">to save the\nphenomena</a>\" or \"<a href=\"http://books.google.com/books?id=UofBybolmREC&amp;lpg=PP1&amp;pg=PA131#v=onepage&amp;q&amp;f=false\">σώζειν τὰ φαινόμενα</a>\" to describe how various\ntheories can \"save\" or \"account for\" the same sensible appearances (cf.\n<a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scientific_formalism\">scientific formalism</a>).</p></li>\n</ul>\n\n<p>Cf. <a href=\"http://philosophy.stackexchange.com/q/3916/2014\">this thread</a> and <a href=\"https://philosophy.stackexchange.com/questions/3916/regarding-platonism-and-the-absurd-applicability-of-mathematics-to-physics/3933#comment38853_3933\">these comments</a> for background to this question. This question also relates to the <a href=\"https://philosophy.stackexchange.com/questions/9940/structural-realism-vs-scientific-formalism\">\"Structural Realism vs. Scientific Formalism\" debate</a>.</p>\n"},{"link":"http://philosophy.stackexchange.com/questions/16038/if-the-platonic-world-exists-how-would-we-know","title":"If the Platonic world exists how would we know?","creationDate":1411153929,"checked":false,"isBad":false,"isGood":false,"site":"philosophy","questionID":16038,"body":"<p>If we assume existence of a non-material world of ideas that mathematics describes there are some questions that a Platonist has to address. </p>\n\n<p>1) How is the ideal world related to the real one, where mathematics also plays a role?</p>\n\n<p>2) How do we gain access to the ideal world and establish truths about it with \"absolute certainty\" in mathematics?</p>\n\n<p>Plato's answer to the first question was that the real things imperfectly \"imitate\" ideal originals, like shadows on a wall. His answer to the second was even more creative. Before birth our soul contemplates the ideal world directly, but forgets the experience upon birth. Interacting with imitations of ideas jogs our memory of them leading to the ideal truths, the process he called anamnesis (unforgetting). While <a href=\"http://philosophy.stackexchange.com/questions/8219/archaic-mathematical-reality-as-referred-to-by-mathematician-alain-connes\">modern Platonists</a> may accept the imitation theory I doubt that many of them would subscribe to anamnesis. Cleared of fantastic elements it essentially equips us with a version of \"mindsight\", a sixth sense that reveals the ideal world directly, unlike the other five. There is no evidence in the work of mathematicians that we possess such a thing, which is probably why Plato moved his mindsight to pre-birth. And if this mindsight is intuition then it is a rather unreliable source. </p>\n\n<p>On the other hand, if we do not have direct access to the ideal and only reconstruct it from the imperfect reality then there is a problem. Not only can't we be absolutely certain that our reconstructions establish truths about it, we can't even be sure they reflect it at all. Leibniz expanded the imitation theory to \"pre-established harmony\" between the ideal, the material, and our mind, which exists because \"God creates the best and most harmonious world\". But this is no better than mindsight. And it gets worse. All we actually have to go on then are our interactions with reality and the process of reconstruction. If we can get to ideas from that the Platonic world and the pre-established harmony are not just speculations, they are unnecessary complications, superfluous like ether in relativity.</p>\n\n<p>I am not very familiar with more recent mathematical Platonism, especially in the 20th century, <a href=\"http://plato.stanford.edu/entries/platonism-mathematics/#Abs\" rel=\"nofollow\">Stanford article</a> is more about objections to Platonism than arguments in its favor. But it seems to remain popular with mathematicians, perhaps some philosophers too, so I am curious.</p>\n\n<blockquote>\n  <p>How does modern Platonism explain our ability to acquire knowledge about the ideal world? What is the argument for not cutting the Platonic world with Occam's razor?</p>\n</blockquote>\n\n<p>EDIT: Vow, this is not what I expected. I originally hoped that a Platonist, or someone familiar with modern Platonism, would make the best case for the ideal world while accounting for more recent realizations, like fallibility of intuition and Kant's critisism of metaphysics. But it seems that all answers essentially concede non-existence of the ideal world, and either make an emotion/motivation based arguments for Platonism \"in practice only\", or reinterpret ideas conceptualistically. Mathematics as it exists is a study of the most schematic properties of physical objects, it has both theoretical and empirical aspects. If our world consisted of unstable shapes constantly morphing into each other we would have a very different kind of geometry, if the objects we encounter sprouted new ones when put together we would have a very different kind of arithmetic and set theory. </p>\n\n<p>Mathematics doesn't study some \"arbitrary\" systems of axioms, it never did and it never will. Which systems are \"good\" or \"interesting\" is discovered by trial and error, just as theoretical parts in all sciences. Sure, mathematics is unfalsifiable in the sense that if you accept the axioms (and logic) you have to accept the theorems, but Newtonian mechanics is unfalsifiable in exactly the same sense. And in the sense that it is falsifiable so is Euclidean geometry. I suppose, one can imagine a being doing mathematics without sensory input, but one can also imagine a being that creates new worlds as it pleases with their own laws of physics. </p>\n\n<p>Scientific theories are not verified or falsified by experiments alone either, as Einstein remarked \"only theory decides what one observes\". String theory gives a stark example of just how indirect a relation to observations and experiments can be for a vibrant physical theory. Mathematics is the most theoretical of sciences, but there are more commonalities between it and physics than between physics and say psychology, so it seems to be well within the spectrum.</p>\n\n<blockquote>\n  <p>Is there a separation in modern philosophy between mathematics as practiced and \"ideal mathematics\" that \"has no bearing on the physical world and cannot be affected by it\"? What is the philosophical basis for such separation, is there similar separation for other sciences? Is the controversial status of mathematics as a science due to historical tradition of Plato, Kant and Husserl who treated its source as metaphysically different?</p>\n</blockquote>\n\n<p>EDIT: Euclidean and non-Euclidean geometry are not \"arbitrary\" at all, one summarizes our basic space experience, the other modifies only one aspect of it which may not even be noticable at small distances. Grothendieck invented etale cohomology to prove Weil conjectures about algebraic varieties, following up on that quickly leads to tangible things as well. Even the purest of areas like logic, set theory or algebra can be easily traced to their roots, including Godel's theorems with their reliance on arithmetic and \"effective axiomatization\". I will try to explain what I mean. Korsgaard writes (p.7):</p>\n\n<blockquote>\n  <p>And this explains Socrates’s puzzling definition of justice. Justice, he says, is “doing one’s own work and not meddling with what isn’t one’s own” (433a–b). When Socrates first introduces this principle into the discussion (369eff.), he’s talking about the specialization of labor, and that’s what the principle sounds like it’s about. But if we think of the constitution as laying out the procedures for deliberative action, and the roles and offices that constitute those procedures, we can see what Socrates’s point is. For usurping the office of another in the constitutional procedures for collective action is precisely what we mean by injustice, or at least it is one thing we mean. For instance <strong>if the constitution says that the president cannot make war without the agreement of the congress, and yet he does, he has usurped congress’s role in this decision, and that’s unjust. If the constitution says that each citizen gets to cast one vote in the election, and through some fraud you manage to vote more than once, you are diminishing the voice of others in the election, and that’s unjust</strong>. So injustice, in one of its most familiar senses, is usurping the role of another in the deliberative procedures that define collective action. It is meddling with somebody else’s work.</p>\n</blockquote>\n\n<p>Now, considering that the <em>polis</em> Plato has in mind is arranged perfectly, and thus <em>just</em>, somebody who \"meddles with what isn't her own\" is being unjust. But having bought that, are we already, without further steps, buying that the same goes for acting against the constitution of, say, the US? Doesn't the assessment of a person's act as <em>unjust</em> mainly depend on the installation of <em>justice</em> in the society that person is acting in? For that would mean that it might not be unjust to non-conform with the constitution of an unjust society. </p>\n\n<p>If I read the following correctly, then it's not Korsgaard's goal to tell us how to behave in a society. She tells us that to conform to a constitution means to make collective action possible in the first place, and later she will, just as Plato, transfer this back to the person and its faculties/soul. Nonetheless, I wonder if her modernisation of Plato can carry the argument. Therefore the question: Is usurping the office of another in a society that might be described as not yet just <em>unjust</em> in the same way it would be in the perfect <em>polis</em>? I don't think so. </p>\n\n<p>My question would be: Does anybody know whether Korsgaard herself motivated this step somewhere else, maybe in <em>Self-Constituion: Agency, Identity, and Integrity</em>?
