Even though the original arguments for the soul were not religious (e.g. Plato and Aristotle, and more recently Descartes, Leibniz, and even more recently Thomas Nagel), we still think the scientific revolution somehow made the soul an incoherent idea.</p>\n\n<p>Based on discussions, it seems there are a couple key elements that make the soul outdated.  First, we now can pinpoint certain ways in which the brain influences how we think, feel, perceive, and we can even read rudimentary thoughts from the brain.  It is certainly the case that the brain is tied to mind states in some form or fashion.</p>\n\n<p>Second, computers provide a compelling and mathematically precise model for how minds can be generated from brains.  We have been able to replace many disciplines with computers which were thought restricted to human capabilities.  Even much of our popular music today is largely computer generated.  </p>\n\n<p>If this trend continues, then we could replace humans entirely with computers.  If a computer is indistinguishable from a human, then even if such a thing as a soul does exist, a computationally simulated soul is good enough for most folks (see Tupac hologram).  </p>\n\n<p>Finally, disciplines that were considered primarily concerned with the soul, such as religion and the humanities, are becoming more and more irrelevant and counter to our modern way of life.  Instead, we try to explain everything with science.  If the soul isn't needed to understand our everyday lives, what does it matter?</p>\n\n<p>Yet, given all these scientific, technical and social forces that are pushing the soul into irrelevance, they still do not amount to a secular disproof of the soul, insofar as we can talk of proofs and disproofs of such ideas.</p>\n\n<p>So, is it possible to adapt the old-fashioned secular arguments for the soul to our modern times? (Phaedo 75a)","creationDate":1395656816,"checked":false,"isBad":false,"isGood":false,"site":"philosophy","questionID":10386,"body":"<p><em>Phaedo</em> 74e-75b</p>\n\n<blockquote>\n  <p>“Then we must have had knowledge of equality before the time when we first saw equal things and thought, ‘All these things are aiming to be like equality but fall short.’”</p>\n  \n  <p>“That is true.”</p>\n  \n  <p>\"And we agree, also, that we have not gained knowledge of it, and that\n  it is impossible to gain this knowledge, except by sight or touch or\n  some other of the senses? I consider that all the senses are alike.”</p>\n  \n  <p>“Yes, Socrates, they are all alike, for the purposes of our argument.”</p>\n  \n  <p>“Then it is through the senses that we must learn that all sensible\n  objects strive after absolute equality and fall short of it. Is that\n  our view?”</p>\n  \n  <p>“Yes.”</p>\n  \n  <p>“Then before we began to see or hear or use the other senses we must\n  somewhere have gained a knowledge of abstract or absolute equality, if\n  we were to compare with it the equals which we perceive by the senses,\n  and see that all such things yearn to be like abstract equality but\n  fall short of it.”</p>\n</blockquote>\n\n<p>[Taken from <a href=\"http://www.perseus.tufts.edu/hopper/text?doc=Perseus%3Atext%3A1999.01.0170%3Atext%3DPhaedo%3Asection%3D75a\" rel=\"nofollow\">http://www.perseus.tufts.edu/hopper/text?doc=Perseus%3Atext%3A1999.01.0170%3Atext%3DPhaedo%3Asection%3D75a</a>]</p>\n\n<p>I feel like I'm misunderstanding something here. Socrates says, \"we have not gained knowledge of it, and... it is impossible to gain this knowledge, except by... the senses.\" What does \"it\" refer to?</p>\n\n<p>Later he says, \"... before we began to... use the... senses we must have gained a knowledge of abstract or absolute equality...\"</p>\n\n<p>This seems to show that \"it\" wasn't referring to abstract equality, because Socrates has stated that we must have gained knowledge of abstract equality without using physical senses. \nThis would make me seem that \"it\" might then be referring to equal things (that fall short of abstract equality), because these are the two things that were being discussed. But if \"it\" refers to equal things, then how have we not gained knowledge of it? We have gained knowledge of it through seeing it.</p>\n\n<p>Am I missing something, or is there a contradiction?</p>\n"},{"link":"http://philosophy.stackexchange.com/questions/10262/contradiction-and-the-being-and-becoming-of-mathematics","title":"Contradiction, and the Being and Becoming of Mathematics","creationDate":1395032281,"checked":false,"isBad":false,"isGood":false,"site":"philosophy","questionID":10262,"body":"<p>Mathematics is rife with contradictions, is shot through with them: the fault-lines lie where theories collide, fade or open up.</p>\n\n<p>Does this disturb the incarnation of mathematics - the Ideal Mathematician? </p>\n\n<p>The Ideal Mathematician is not. His inward calm being the Being of Mathematics, his third eye cast on the third realm - the third realm of pure abstractions. Unique in themselves and as Singular as crystals and more Splendid; the shadows of which treads his own human mind - the secondary realm of cognition and the intellect. </p>\n\n<p>(Can one say, that mathematics is <em>found</em> in the primary world, the realm of actual physical being? Possibly it is <em>obtained</em> as a correlation between them).</p>\n\n<p>From the Purity of the third realm, comes the light, that places all things in their correct station and gravity, for in the Third Realm, it is already as <em>such</em>. For in the Third Realm contradiction does not <em>obtain</em>.</p>\n\n<p>Thus that Spiritus Mundi of Mathematics - Platonism as Plato did not put it but as co-opted - is not troubled by vast images of discord and diction, they are illusions of  our too-human mind. </p>\n\n<p>Say, as a <em>hypothetical</em>, that Platonism is also an illusion, that the Being of mathematics is not-there. Then what is left? Not its Being, but its Becoming, and its Becoming must involve contradictions <em>essentially</em>. For there is no appeal to the Ideal to whisk them away. And these contradictions are not on the frontier of mathematics (were we to imagine mathematics as some vast ascending Sphere) but reach back right in and through and back and in through the body of mathematics (though we have banished the Body) - in every place and in every direction. Thus mathematics as Becoming unfolds.  </p>\n\n<p>Here is the Event of Mathematics - its adventure.  </p>\n\n<p>Question:</p>\n\n<p>Does denying the Being of Mathematics (Platonism) leads necessarily to <em>pure</em> Becoming, and in the Being of its Becoming (and not the Becoming of its Being) are contradictions <em>essential</em> - that is irreducibly, inerradicably irremovable?</p>\n\n<p><strong>Coda</strong></p>\n\n<p>Given some of the comments about the obscurity and opacity of the language, I thought it might be useful to 'explain' the question.</p>\n\n<p>The mainstream ontology, I take it, of mathematics is Platonism where abstractions like the number '2', or the group 'Z x Z' exist; but moreover that propositions about these objects also exist with well defined truth-values. That is the proposition '2 is an even number'; and further that theories themselves also exist, like PA with first-order classical logic. The Platonic realm, is considered to exist outside of space &amp; time. I also consider it mainstream that the law of the excluded middle or that contradictions are possible in this realm are not possible. (Notably, in Aristotles discussion of the law of contradiction, he left open the possiblity of what kind of truth value one can assign to a proposition that refers to the future. But in the Platonic realm there is no time, so no future).</p>\n\n<p>I ask, suppose that Platonism isn't true, and that at least time is inherently involved in the ontology of mathematical objects, if not also space. One might position it as taking the epistemology of mathematics as its ontology.</p>\n\n<p>I then discuss the role of the law of contradictions when this is done. One might say that what contradiction means in epistemological terms is different from what it means ontologically. And I'm proposing that contradictions are essential epistemologically; because unlike Platonism, where truths are exhibited all at once; epistemologicaly, there are different theories, some of which whose propositions or theorems they may have in common, others may contradict. It may be true, that in the process of <em>time</em>, one may bring these theories in line with each other, but I also expect that very movement also brings into view other theories which are incommensurable.</p>\n\n<p>I pick-up Whiteheads terminology for the Platonic realm - the third realm and take the first &amp; second to be Descartes picture of the world as being divided into physical &amp; mental substances. I refer to the ontology of Mathematics, as its Being, alluding to Badious conception of Mathematics as the 'very site of ontology', which is a resucitation of Platos ontology of Forms, but with the Forms as considered as abstract objects - this is (very) different from mathematical Platonism.</p>\n\n<p>That I talk about Purity and Light is an allusion to the emanationist philosophy of Plotinus which is indebted to Platos Philosophy; but also interpreted in the question as the 'lack of contradictions', and also as a metaphor for the worlds to influence each other; but also so I can bring in Platos famous picture of his Forms casting 'shadows' in the real world.</p>\n\n<p>Becoming is to Being in Continental thought as the Heracleitian Flux is to Platos Forms. Hegel refers to Becoming as the sublation of Being &amp; non-Being, and Heidegger when thinking of Being qua Being, identifies Being with time, that is Becoming.</p>\n\n<p>Though I mention the 'Event' of Mathematics, its 'Adventure' as where new ideas 'collide, fade or open up' and refers to Mathematics as an active and creative process, its not the main intent of the question. </p>\n"},{"link":"http://philosophy.stackexchange.com/questions/10211/gut-and-toe-as-fallacies-of-misplaced-concreteness","title":"GUT and TOE as Fallacies of Misplaced Concreteness?","creationDate":1394651537,"checked":false,"isBad":false,"isGood":false,"site":"philosophy","questionID":10211,"body":"<p>A.N. Whitehead warns in the introduction to <em>Process and Reality</em>, that the “chief error” of Western philosophy is “overstatement.” He states: “the aim at generalization is sound, but the estimate of success is exaggerated” (<em>PR</em>, 1978, 7). Further, Whitehead is quick to point out many instances in the history of science and philosophy in which public opinion was dominated by dogmas of “misplaced concreteness,” that is abstractions or hypothetical ideas mistaken as concrete actual entities. This appears to be the common practice of scientists who believe they are on to a Grand Unified Theory (GUT) or Theory of Everything (TOE), which involves equations of strong and weak nuclear force, gravity, and quantum physics. Unlike Whitehead, they claim to speak for all epochs rather than our “immediate” one. </p>\n\n<p>But this ignores the problem of measurement and the qualifications that guide our observations and data—for Whitehead, the mathematical order full of genuine possibilities is subordinated to the act of measuring. He writes: </p>\n\n<blockquote>\n  <p>all exact measurements concern perceptions in the mode of\n  presentational imme¬diacy [space]; and that such observations purely\n  concern the systematic geometric forms of the environment, forms\n  defined by projectors from the ‘seat’ of the strain and irrespective\n  of the actualities which constitute the environment. The contemporary\n  actualities of the world are irrelevant to these observations. All\n  scientific measurements merely concern the sys¬tematic real\n  potentiality out of which these actualities arise. This is the meaning\n  of the doctrine that physical science is solely concerned with the\n  mathematical relations of the world (<em>PR</em>, 326).</p>\n</blockquote>\n\n<p>Whitehead has a Theory of Extension (Part IV of <em>PR</em>) which holds space and extension are not the same and, like Plato’s Receptacle in the <em>Timaeus</em>, all temporal and spatial relations depend on the “undivided divisibility” of the “extensive continuum.” The extensive continuum symbolizes the universe as such, without denying the regionalization of our experience. </p>\n\n<p>Any system of measurement presupposes certain regions or “neighborhoods” of relations under the environmental conditions of the dominant nexūs of societies. Measurement is shot through with the perception of “presentational immediacy” which “depends solely on the ‘withness’ of the ‘body,’ and only exhibits the external contemporary world in respect to its systematic geometrical relationship to the ‘body’” (<em>PR</em>, 334). Are GUT and TOE exaggerated instances of using abstractions to explain concrete relatedness? Is Whitehead correct to suggest that “measurement is a systematic procedure dependent on the dominant so¬cieties of the cosmic epoch,” which “depend on the actual occasions which atomize the extensive con¬tinuum” (<em>PR</em>, 332)?        </p>\n"},{"link":"http://philosophy.stackexchange.com/questions/9854/my-professor-claimed-that-f-ma-is-a-platonic-form-i-disagreed-but-is-it","title":"My professor claimed that &quot;F = ma&quot; is a Platonic form. I disagreed, but is it?","creationDate":1392258507,"checked":false,"isBad":false,"isGood":false,"site":"philosophy","questionID":9854,"body":"<p>My professor claimed in a lecture that equations such as \"Force = mass x acceleration\" are Platonic forms. I disagree for a few reasons:</p>\n\n<p>A form is non-spatiotemporal. Force is a vector, which has magnitude and direction. In mathematics, vectors describe space. </p>\n\n<p>Force = dp/dt. That is, it is the change in momentum per change in time. It is dependent on time.</p>\n\n<p>Furthermore, a force is independent of human reality. Newton's equation is dependent on the use of the Euclidean metric. But on another planet, that society could use a completely different metric. For instance, the discrete metric or the taxicab metric, which would make their equation for force completely different. </p>\n\n<p>He's also said some other irksome things, such as the Pythagorean Theorem (a^2 + b^2 = c^2) is a Platonic form. I also disagree with this one.</p>\n\n<p>The Pythagorean Theorem depends on the acceptance of Euclid's Fifth postulate - the parallel postulate. But there is not universal agreement that our universe is best described by Euclidean geometry. </p>\n\n<p>There are competing geometries such as Lobachevskian geometry (geometry on a sphere) or Riemannian geometry. These are gained by taking the negation of Euclid's postulate.</p>\n\n<p>So I don't understand how equations such as these can be Platonic forms - that is, non-spatiotemporal ideals independent of human realities.</p>\n\n<p>What are your thoughts?</p>\n"},{"link":"http://philosophy.stackexchange.com/questions/9834/is-an-exegete-an-explicator","title":"Is an exegete an explicator?","creationDate":1392159680,"checked":false,"isBad":false,"isGood":false,"site":"philosophy","questionID":9834,"body":"<p>The SEP, remarks that, Ion in Platos dialogue of the same name:</p>\n\n<blockquote>\n  <p>is a prize-winning professional reciter of poetry—a “rhapsode” — and of Homer in particular. Though he speaks his lines with the requisite conviction and emotion, he does not “imitate” his subjects in the sense of act their parts (of course, Homer did not write for the stage). He is a performer but not a (stage) actor. </p>\n  \n  <p>Ion is depicted as superb at making the Iliad and Odyssey come <em>alive</em>, at communicating their <em>drama</em> to his audience and at involving them <em>intimately</em>. We might say that he “represents” or “expresses” the characters, action, and narrative of Homer's epic poems, and thus in some sense both identifies with his subject and leads his audience to do the same. </p>\n</blockquote>\n\n<p>The SEP goes on to say:</p>\n\n<blockquote>\n  <p>If Ion is an exegete or explicator of Homer's poems, he must surely understand what the poet means, else he could not explain the poet's thoughts. This seemingly commonsensical point is asserted by Socrates at the start, and happily accepted by Ion. </p>\n</blockquote>\n\n<p>One ought to be suspicious of seemingly innocuous statements. And this supposition deserves at least some looking at.</p>\n\n<p>As poetry is an obscure taste in the contemporary world - let us take instead the art of driving, in particular the art of driving formula-1 cars. Does Ayrton Senna understand the vehicle better than the mechanics who built it? Two obvious propositions present themselves:</p>\n\n<p>a. One can say at least Senna could not have built the car as well as the mechanics. </p>\n\n<p>b. That the mechanics could not have driven the car as well as Senna.</p>\n\n<p>One can say that driving the car as Senna does, is to see the full expression of the vehicle in its aspect of speed and panache: it is to understand the car as <em>use</em>. To understand the car as highly-tuned internal combustion engine is to understand a car in its analytic and mechanic aspect. The two do not appear to be commensurate on only these two grounds - a third ground has to be introduced. </p>\n\n<p>If one was to ask Senna how he drove so well, one supposes he knows in the sense of having or embodying know-how, but does not know in the sense of articulating precisely as one would expect a mechanic to explain the intricate functioning of an engine.</p>\n\n<p>One might say Senna is an exegete of the car. And the mechanic its explicator.</p>\n\n<p>In like analogy, can one say that Ion embodies know-how but not have knowing-how? </p>\n\n<p>That his knowledge, that is his attitude to what is under question, pace Heidegger, is <em>ready-to-hand</em> and not <em>present-to-hand</em>?</p>\n"},{"link":"http://philosophy.stackexchange.com/questions/9790/are-numbers-universal-in-set-theory-and-nominalist-in-category-theory","title":"Are numbers universal in Set Theory and nominalist in Category Theory?","creationDate":1391828471,"checked":false,"isBad":false,"isGood":false,"site":"philosophy","questionID":9790,"body":"<p>The number 3, when considered as a universal, abstracts the property of 3'ness from all groups of 3 objects. One supposes that this universal, by conceptual neccessity of what is understood by the idea of 'universal' is unique. The problem of universals is the ontological status of this universal which can be broadly characterised as being of three kinds - real (following Plato), nominal (following Ockham) and Ideal (following Kant). </p>\n\n<p>In the usual theory of Sets, one notices, that for example the empty set by the axiom of extensionality is unique.</p>\n\n<p>But what about the number 3 in theory of Sets?</p>\n\n<p>If one follows a particular construction of the integers, for example by Von Neumanns nested sets, then the number 3 is determined and constructed uniquely. But one can always choose another construction. And one notes that the detailed construction of the integers do not matter - at least where one is not concerned with their ontological status - but how they function in a theory which makes use of certain of their properties.</p>\n\n<p>One might say, rather than use one particular construction amongst many, use all possible constructions.</p>\n\n<p>In this sense, the number 3 appears to lose its universality and appears in the world of concrete objects. That is the number 3 is thought of not as the instantiation of the abstract property of 3'ness shared by all groups of 3 objects, but as  <em>all</em> groups of three objects but understood both indefinately and particularly. Indefinate as we choose not some represenative amongst them, and particularly because we keep hold of their distinctness.</p>\n\n<p>Is this a form of nominalism?</p>\n\n<p>What I've described above is how numbers are understood in Topos Theory - aka generalised Set Theory.</p>\n\n<ol>\n<li><p>Can one say, at least roughly, that Set Theory attempts to ground itself on Platonic Realism, which states that universals exists. And that Category/Topos Theory attempts to ground itself on nominalism?</p></li>\n<li><p>That grounding of Set Theory on Platonic Realism appears to be a failure for the reasons I've given above: In that the only universal it contains is the empty set, and that it doesn't provide us with the universal example of the number 3? </p></li>\n<li><p>That the attempt to ground the the uniqueness that should be inherent in the realist position on universals, at least in set theory, is/was understood to be captured by the axiom of extensionality. One notices in particular that the uniqueness of the empty set is essentially reliant on this axiom.</p></li>\n<li><p>Does Category/Topos Theory capture the nominalist position - or are there failures here? One notes that the theory speaks of 'universal properties' which has a precise technical definition, and one notes that they do not produce a unique object but simply characterises those objects that have those properties - and show, as one should readily note, that any two such objects, so far as these properties are under consideration, are equivalent.  </p></li>\n</ol>\n"},{"link":"http://philosophy.stackexchange.com/questions/9656/is-platos-theory-of-the-forms-entirely-accepted-by-anyone","title":"Is Plato&#39;s Theory of the Forms entirely accepted by anyone?","creationDate":1391174035,"checked":false,"isBad":false,"isGood":false,"site":"philosophy","questionID":9656,"body":"<p>Plato's idea:\n\"Plato's theory of Forms or theory of Ideas asserts that non-material abstract (but substantial) forms (or ideas), and not the material world of change known to us through sensation, possess the highest and most fundamental kind of reality.\"- WIKIPEDIA.\nEven the Church (all branches of...) seem to only take 'some' of the Theory. It tries to prove life is generated from death, just as waking from sleeping. It states if life goes into death but death never back into life, then there will be no life and all things will remain in the state of death.</p>\n\n<p>Is the assumption that all opposites are generated out of each other reasonable? </p>\n\n<blockquote>\n  <p>And that these admissions were not unfair, Cebes, he said, may be shown, I think, as follows: If generation were in a straight line only, and there were no compensation or circle in nature, no turn or return of elements into their opposites, then you know that all things would at last have the same form and pass into the same state, and there would be no more generation of them.</p>\n  \n  <p>What do you mean? he said.</p>\n  \n  <p>A simple thing enough, which I will illustrate by the case of sleep, he replied. subprogram resides in a separate\n  file from the  main program, it must be compiled separately. Under\n  FTN90 an intermediate type of machine code, called relocatable binary\n  is produced in a file with the .OBJ extension. This in turn must be\n  linked with the calling program by means of a special program called a\n  linker, finally resulting in a .EXE version of the main program. For good measure, the List is then .Clear()-ed.</p>\n\n<p>After cleaning the slate, holder is deep copied back into skillPanels, which is then iterated over it and adds its members to the larger <code>Panel</code>, positioning each by hand as I don't trust layout managers as far as I can throw them. This level of circuitousness is sadly need for a for-each-FlowLayoutPanel remove method that will be added in the future. function thets(t,p)\nc**  Function calculates thetaEsaturate given t and p\n      common /params/xkap,pr,tr,xl,cp,rgas,grav,taue,taus,taup,tauc\n      if(t.lt.273.15) then\n      es=esice(t)\n p(ilx),xlp(ilx),dlp(ilx),dp(ilx)\n      write(*,*) 'Setting Pressure Levels'\n      write(*,*) '    i    p(i)    dp(i)    logp      dlogp'\n      pmin=2000.\n      pmax=101300.\n dp(i)=p(i+1)-p(i)\n      end do\n      dlp(ilx)=0.0\n      do i=1,ilx\n      write(*,*) i,p(i),dp(i),xlp(i),dlp(i)\n      end do\n      return\n      x(i)=0.0\n      end do\n      return\n      end\n\nC#######################################################################\n\n    FUNCTION ESICE(TK)                                                      \n\nC   THIS FUNCTION RETURNS THE SATURATION VAPOR PRESSURE WITH RESPECT TO \nC   ICE ESICE (Pascals) GIVEN THE TEMPERATURE T (Kelvin). DLH 11.19.97\nC   THE FORMULA USED IS BASED UPON THE INTEGRATION OF THE CLAUSIUS-     \nC   CLAPEYRON EQUATION BY GOFF AND GRATCH.  THE FORMULA APPEARS ON P.350\nC   OF THE SMITHSONIAN METEOROLOGICAL TABLES, SIXTH REVISED EDITION,    \nC   1963.                                                               \n\n    DATA CTA,EIS/273.15,6.1071/                                            \n\nC   CTA = DIFFERENCE BETWEEN KELVIN AND CELSIUS TEMPERATURE             \nC   EIS = SATURATION VAPOR PRESSURE (MB) OVER A WATER-ICE MIXTURE AT 0C \n\n    DATA C1,C2,C3/9.09718,3.56654,0.876793/                                \n\nC   C1,C2,C3 = EMPIRICAL COEFFICIENTS IN THE GOFF-GRATCH FORMULA        \nc**** Convert to Celsius\nc        tc=t-273.15\n    IF (TK.LE.CTA) GO TO 5                                                   \n    ESICE = 99999.                                                         \n    WRITE(6,3)ESICE                                                        \n    3   FORMAT(' SATURATION VAPOR PRESSURE FOR ICE CANNOT BE COMPUTED',   \n     1         /' FOR TEMPERATURE &gt; 0C. ESICE =',F7.0)                   \n    RETURN                                                                 \n    5   CONTINUE                                                          \n\nC   FREEZING POINT OF WATER (K)                                         \n\n    TF = CTA                                                               \n\nC   GOFF-GRATCH FORMULA                                                 \n\n    RHS = -C1*(TF/TK-1.)-C2*ALOG10(TF/TK)+C3*(1.-TK/TF)+ALOG10(EIS)        \n    ESI = 10.**RHS                                                         \n    IF (ESI.LT.0.) (AsyncTask):</p>\n\n<pre><code>public class Background extends AsyncTask&lt;Long, Void, Void&gt; \n{\n// Variables\nString color;\nButton button;\n\npublic Background(Button button)\n{\n {\n\n}\n</code></pre>\n\n<p>}</p>\n"},{"link":"http://stackoverflow.com/questions/22347050/exclude-all-files-from-folder-for-plato-metric","title":"Exclude all files from folder for plato id=\"link2H_INTR\"&gt;&lt;!--  H2 anchor --&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/p&gt;\n&lt;h2 id=\"pgepubid00003\"&gt;INTRODUCTION AND ANALYSIS.&lt;/h2&gt;\n&lt;p&gt;The Republic of Plato is the longest of his works with the exception of the Laws, and is certainly the greatest of them. There are nearer approaches to modern metaphysics in the Philebus and in the Sophist; the Politicus or Statesman is more ideal; the form and institutions of the State are more clearly drawn out in the Laws; as works of art, the Symposium and the Protagoras are of higher excellence. But no other Dialogue of Plato has the same largeness of view and the same perfection of style; no other shows an equal knowledge of the world, or contains more of those thoughts which are new as well as old, and not of one age only but of all. Nowhere in Plato is there a deeper irony or a greater wealth of humour or imagery, or more dramatic power. Nor in any other of his writings is the attempt made to interweave life and speculation, or to connect politics with philosophy. The Republic is the centre around which the other Dialogues may be grouped; here philosophy reaches the highest point (cp, especially in Books V, VI, VII) to which ancient thinkers ever attained. Plato among the Greeks, like Bacon among the moderns, was the first who conceived a method of knowledge, although neither of them always distinguished the bare outline or form from the substance of truth; and both of them had to be content with an abstraction of science which was not yet realized. He was the greatest metaphysical genius whom the world has seen; and in him, more than in any other ancient thinker, the germs of future knowledge are contained. The sciences of logic and psychology, which have supplied so many instruments of thought to after-ages, are based upon the analyses of Socrates and Plato. The principles of definition, the law of contradiction, the fallacy of arguing in a circle, the distinction between the essence and accidents of a thing or notion, between means and ends, between causes and conditions; also the division of the mind into the rational, concupiscent, and irascible elements, or of pleasures and desires into necessary and unnecessary—these and other great forms of thought are all of them to be found in the Republic, and were probably first invented by Plato. The greatest of all logical truths, and the one of which writers on philosophy are most apt to lose sight, the difference between words and things, has been most strenuously insisted on by him (cp. Rep.; Polit.; Cratyl), although he has not always avoided the confusion of them in his own writings (e.g. Rep.). But he does not bind up truth in logical formulae,—logic is still veiled in metaphysics; and the science which he imagines to 'contemplate all truth and all existence' is very unlike the doctrine of the syllogism which Aristotle claims to have discovered (Soph. Elenchi).&lt;/p&gt;\n&lt;/body&gt;&lt;/html&gt;\n</code></pre>\n"},{"link":"http://stackoverflow.com/questions/20643361/remove-rows-from-data-frame-if-values-added-together-is-less-than-x","title":"Remove rows from data frame if values added together is less than x","creationDate":1387309791,"checked":false,"isBad":true,"isGood":false,"site":"stackoverflow","questionID":20643361,"body":"<p>I have the following data frame, call it df, which is a data frame consisting in three vectors: \"Scene,\" \"Name,\" and \"Appearances.\"  I would like to total the value for \"Appearances\" for every instance in which the \"Name\" is in the list and divide it by the number of times the name appears in the list. lenses with Control.Lens where an intermediate function must be called","creationDate":1378156920,"checked":false,"isBad":true,"isGood":false,"site":"stackoverflow","questionID":18580916,"body":"<p>Suppose I have the following:</p>\n\n<pre><code>{-# LANGUAGE TemplateHaskell #-}\n\nimport Control.Lens\n\ndata Book = Book {\n  _author :: String,\n  _title :: String\n} deriving (Show)\n\nmakeLenses ''Book\n\ndata Location = Location {\n  _city :: String,\n  _state :: String\n} deriving (Show)\n\nmakeLenses ''Location\n\ndata Library = Library {\n  _location :: Location,\n  _books :: [Book]\n} deriving (Show)\n\nmakeLenses ''Library\n\nlib :: Library\nlib = Library (Location \"Baltimore\" \"MD\") [Book \"Plato\" \"Republic\", Book \"Aristotle\" \"Ethics\"]\n</code></pre>\n\n<p>I'm trying to understand various ways of reaching down through multiple layers by composing lenses. I know how to do these operations:</p>\n\n<pre><code>-- returns \"Baltimore\"\nlib ^. location . city\n\n-- returns a copy of lib with the city replaced\nset (location . city) \"Silver Spring\" lib\n</code></pre>\n\n<p>But what if I want to change book titles?
